Building vs. Bequeathing a Church: Abu Hanifa's Distinction
Abu Hanifa's distinction: building does not transfer ownership, bequest does

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a dhimmi's church remains inheritable property under Abu Hanifa, while a bequest for a church transfers ownership.

Core Idea
Construction does not transfer ownership; only proper dedication (tahriz) or a valid bequest removes the builder's ownership.

This passage presents a key legal distinction made by Imam Abu Hanifa regarding church property owned by a dhimmi. The ruling hinges on the concept of tahriz—making a property exclusive for Allah. Understanding this distinction is vital for correctly determining inheritance outcomes and for grasping the Hanafi approach to property dedication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text contrasts building a church/synagogue on one's land with bequeathing it. Note that both scenarios involve a dhimmi testator.
Bequest is a transfer of ownership after death; building is a physical act of construction.
2
Understand the principle of tahriz
Ownership is removed only when the property becomes muḥraz (exclusively dedicated) for Allah, like a mosque. This requires absence of any human use rights.
Tahriz is stricter than mere intention; it must be free of competing claims.
3
Apply the principle to building a church
A church is not truly muḥraz for Allah because it often includes living quarters and is used partially for non-worship purposes, thus human rights remain attached.
As a result, the builder retains ownership and the building is inherited.
4
Contrast with a bequest for a church
A bequest is a legal instrument designed to transfer ownership after death. For dhimmis, a bequest for a church is considered a qurba (act of devotion) in their belief, so it is valid and ownership transfers away from the testator.
Thus, the bequeathed property is not inherited; it goes to the church.
5
Derive the inheritance outcome
Building: property passes to heirs as part of estate. Bequest: property does not pass to heirs; it becomes the church's property.
The distinction is based on the mechanism: building is not a cause of ownership transfer; bequest is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الفرق لا بى حنيفة بين بناء البيعة والكنيسة وبين الوصية به ان البناء نفسه ليس بسبب لزوال ملک البانى وانما يزول ملكه بان يصير محرزا خالصا الله تعالى كما في مساجد المسلمين والكنيسة لم تصر محرزة الله تعالى حقيقة فتبقى ملكا للبانى فتورث عنه ولانهم يبنون فيها الحجرات و يسكنونها فلم يتحرز لتعلق حق العباد و فى هذه الصورة يورث المسجد ايضا لعدم تحرزه بخلاف الوصية لانه وضع لازالة الملك الا انه امتنع ثبوت مقتضاه في غير ما هو قربة عندهم فبقى فيما هو قربة على مقتضاه فيزول ملكه فلا يورث
ترجمہ پھر ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک بیعہ اور کنیسہ کے بنانے کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ نفس بناء ( تعمیر ) ملک بانی کے زوال کا سبب نہیں ہے اور بانی کی ملک اس طرح زائل ہوتی ہے کہ وہ محرز اور خالص اللہ تعالیٰ کے لئے ہو جائے جیسے مسلمانوں کی مساجد میں اور کنیسہ حقیقہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے لئے محرز نہیں ہوا تو کنیسہ بانی کی ملک برقرار رہے گا تو کنیسہ بالی کی جانب سے میراث میں تقسیم کیا جائے گا۔ اور اس لئے کہ یہ لوگ کنیسہ میں کمرے بناتے ہیں اور ان میں رہتے ہیں تو وہ محرز نہ ہو اس کے ساتھ بندوں کا حق متعلق ہونے کی وجہ
Then the difference according to Abu Hanifa between building a church/synagogue and bequeathing it: The building itself is not a cause for the removal of the builder's ownership. Rather, his ownership is removed only when it becomes a dedicated (muḥraz) thing exclusively for Allah, like in the mosques of Muslims. But a synagogue does not become a thing dedicated exclusively to Allah in reality, so it remains the property of the builder, and is inherited from him. Also, because they build rooms inside synagogues and live in them, it is not fully dedicated due to the involvement of people's rights. In this scenario, even a mosque is inherited if it is not fully dedicated. Unlike a bequest, because it (wasiyya) is established for the removal of ownership, except that its effect is prevented in things that are not a qurba (act of devotion) in their belief, so it remains effective in things that are a qurba, thus his ownership is removed and it is not inherited.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage explains an important legal distinction made by Imam Abu Hanifa between merely building a church/synagogue on one's land and bequeathing the building as a church/synagogue. The key point: constructing a building does not automatically transfer ownership away from the builder. Ownership is transferred only when the property becomes a dedicated (muḥraz) space exclusively for Allah, analogous to Muslim mosques. A synagogue or church, however, is not considered truly dedicated to Allah in the Islamic legal sense because it is not exclusively for divine worship (according to Islamic criteria); moreover, dhimmis often use such buildings partially for residential purposes. Therefore, a church built by a dhimmi remains his property and passes to his heirs. In contrast, a bequest (wasiyya) is specifically designed to transfer ownership after death. For dhimmis, a bequest for a church is valid (as per Abu Hanifa's earlier ruling) because it is a qurba in their belief; thus, ownership transfers and the property does not go to heirs. The commentator notes that even a mosque can be inherited if it is not fully dedicated (muḥraz), but this is a rare case. The distinction underscores the importance of the mechanism (construction vs. bequest) in determining inheritance outcomes.

Key Terms
محرز (muḥraz): A property that is exclusively set apart for a specific purpose (e.g., dedicated to Allah for a mosque), such that it is no longer in the personal ownership of any individual.
وقف (waqf): Not directly mentioned, but the concept of dedication (tahriz) is related; a church is not considered a valid waqf (endowment) in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does a house that is physically built as a church remain part of the builder's estate and get inherited?
A) Because the builder never intended to make it a church permanently.
B) Because the building does not become a dedicated property (muḥraz) exclusively for Allah in the legal sense.
C) Because the bequest was invalid.
D) Because the church was built for an unspecified group.

Key Takeaway
Construction does not transfer ownership; only full dedication (tahriz) or a valid bequest removes the builder's ownership.

First Category of Dhimmi Wills: Qurbah in Their Belief Only
Introduces the first category of dhimmi wills with the pig-slaughter example and the ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the validity of a dhimmi's bequest for an act considered qurbah in the dhimmi's belief but not in Islam.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's will for an act of qurbah in his own religion is valid according to Abu Hanifa, invalid according to the Sahibayn.

In this lesson, we examine the first of four categories of wills made by dhimmis. The key issue is whether a dhimmi can bequeath property for a purpose that is religiously meritorious in his own faith but not in Islam. The Hanafi school is divided on this point, with Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples holding opposite positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
The text states that dhimmi wills are divided into four types. Recognize that this lesson focuses on the first.
The other three categories are not detailed here.
2
Understand the first category
This category covers bequests for acts that are qurbah only in the dhimmi's belief, not in Islam. The example given is slaughtering pigs for polytheists.
Qurbah means an act of religious merit.
3
Note the condition
The bequest is for an unspecified (غير مسمين) group of recipients. This condition triggers the ikhtilaf.
If recipients were specified, the ruling might differ.
4
Recognize the ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa considers such a will valid because the dhimmi's own belief in qurbah suffices. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it invalid because the act is not qurbah in Islam.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the text does not give a tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحاصل ان وصايا الذمى على اربعة اقسام منها ان تكون قربة فى معتقدهم ولا تكون قربة في حقنا وهو ما ذكرناه وما اذا اوصى الذمى بان تذبح خنازیره و تطعم المشركين و هذه على الخلاف اذا كان لقوم غير مسمين كما ذكرنا والوجه ما بيناه
Then the summary is that the wills of the dhimmi are of four types. Among them is that it is a religious act in their belief and not a religious act in our right, and that is what we mentioned. And if the dhimmi bequeaths that his pigs be slaughtered and fed to the polytheists, this is subject to disagreement if it is for an unspecified group as we mentioned, and the reasoning is what we explained.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the first category among four types of dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) wills. The category is a bequest for an act that the dhimmi considers religiously meritorious (qurbah) but Muslims do not. The specific example given is bequeathing that his pigs be slaughtered and fed to polytheists. The author notes that this case involves an intra-Hanafi dispute (علی الخلاف) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Two Companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Imam Abu Hanifa considers such a will valid because he regards the dhimmi's own beliefs as the basis for validity, while the Sahibayn consider it invalid because the act itself is not a qurbah in Islam. The condition mentioned is that the bequest is for an unspecified, non-circumscribed group (غير مسمين); if it were for a specific group, the ruling would differ (as later clarified in the fourth category). The reference 'كما ذكرنا' points to earlier discussion of the proofs for each view. The author does not provide a definitive tarjih here, leaving the ikhtilaf unresolved.

Key Terms
قربة (qurbah): An act considered religiously meritorious or a means of drawing near to God.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state who pays jizyah and enjoys protection.
وصية (wasiyyah): A will or bequest made by a person regarding his property after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first category of dhimmi wills described in the text, what is the ruling if a dhimmi bequeaths that his pigs be slaughtered and fed to a non-specific group of polytheists?
A) Valid by consensus.
B) Invalid by consensus.
C) Valid according to Abu Hanifa, invalid according to Sahibayn.
D) Valid only if the pigs are slaughtered by Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a dhimmi's will for an act of qurbah in his own belief is disputed between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, hinging on whether his own religious framework suffices.

The Second Category of Dhimmi Wills: Qurbah in Our Belief Only
Ruling on second category of dhimmi wills with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for the second category of dhimmi wills and apply the exception for tamlik to known individuals.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's will for an Islamic qurbah is invalid unless it transfers ownership to specific persons.

When a non-Muslim (dhimmi) makes a will, its validity depends on whether the intended act is a religiously meritorious act (qurbah) in their own belief. This passage addresses the second category: acts that are qurbah in Islam but not in the dhimmi's religion. The Hanafi ruling is clear: such wills are invalid by consensus, but with a critical exception that transforms the bequest into a simple transfer of ownership (tamlik). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly advising on dhimmi estates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category and examples
Read the passage and list the three examples given: bequeathing for hajj, building a mosque, lighting lamps in mosques. These are all Islamic acts of worship (qurbah).
The category is defined by the condition that the act is qurbah for Muslims but not for the dhimmi.
2
Understand the basis of the ruling
The ruling hinges on the dhimmi's own religious belief. Since they do not consider these acts as qurbah, their intention (niyyah) for qurbah is absent, and the will is invalid.
Hanafi fiqh requires that the testator intend qurbah for a bequest to be valid as a charitable act (taqrib).
3
Note the ruling and its authority
The passage states the will is 'batilah bi al-ijma' (invalid by consensus). Recognise that this is a strong proof within the Hanafi school.
Ijma here refers to the consensus of Hanafi imams or Companions, not necessarily universal ijma.
4
Recognise the exception and its rationale
The exception is when the bequest is to 'specific individuals' (qawm bi a'yanihim). In that case, the will becomes a tamlik (transfer of ownership), not a dedication for qurbah.
The stated purpose (e.g., build a mosque) is then advisory (mashurah); beneficiaries may use the property otherwise.
5
Apply the rule to new cases
Given a new scenario (e.g., a dhimmi bequeaths money for iftar in a mosque), determine whether it falls under the general invalidity or the exception.
Focus on whether the beneficiaries are specified individuals or an undefined purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اذا اوصى بما يكون قربة في حقنا ولا يكون قربة في معتقدهم كما اذا اوصى بالحج أو بان يبني مسجد المسلمين أو بان يسرج فى مساجد المسلمين وهذه الوصية باطلة بالاجماع اعتباراً لاعتقادهم الا اذا كان لقوم باعيانهم لوقوعه تمليكا لانهم معلومون والجهة مشورة
And among them is when he bequeaths something which is a religious act in our right and not a religious act in their belief, such as when he bequeaths pilgrimage (hajj), or that a mosque for Muslims be built, or that lamps be lit in mosques of Muslims. This will is invalid by consensus, considering their belief, unless it is for specific individuals, because it then takes effect as a tamlik (transfer of ownership) since they are known, and the purpose (jihah) is advisory.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage details the second category of dhimmi wills: the bequest is for an act that is a qurbah in Islam but not in the dhimmi's own religion. Examples include bequeathing funds for hajj, building a mosque, or lighting lamps in a mosque. The ruling is unequivocal: the will is invalid (باطلة) by consensus (بالاجماع) among the Hanafi imams and other scholars, because the dhimmi does not consider these acts as religiously meritorious. The validity of the will depends on the dhimmi's intentional qurbah (قربة). However, there is an exception: if the bequest is made to a specific, identifiable group (قوم بأعيانهم), then it is valid as a transfer of ownership (تمليك). In that case, the beneficiaries become owners of the property, and the stated purpose (e.g., building a mosque) is merely advisory (مشورة), meaning they may use it as they wish. This exception ensures that the will does not lose its effect simply because of the donor's religious background; the actual transfer to known persons validates it.

Key Terms
باطلة بالاجماع (batilah bi al-ijma'): Invalid by consensus of scholars (here, within the Hanafi school).
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership, as opposed to a mere charitable purpose (taqrib).
الجهة مشورة (al-jihah mashurah): The purpose (direction) is advisory; the beneficiary is not obliged to use the property as specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A dhimmi bequeaths money 'for the construction of a mosque' but does not specify any beneficiaries. According to Hanafi fiqh, this will is:
A) Valid because building a mosque is a qurbah in Islam.
B) Invalid because the dhimmi does not believe in the qurbah.
C) Valid if the mosque is in a Muslim-majority country.
D) Invalid only if the dhimmi is wealthy.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's will for an Islamic act of worship is invalid by consensus unless it is a transfer of ownership to specific, known individuals.

Third Category: Qurbah in Both Beliefs
Explains the third category of dhimmi wills which is valid by agreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a dhimmi's bequest of an act considered qurbah in both Islam and the dhimmi's belief, and explain the rationale based on the concept of qurbah haqiqah.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's will for an act that is truly meritorious in both Islam and his own faith is valid by unanimous agreement.

In the previous categories, we saw that a dhimmi's bequest for a purely Islamic qurbah requires conditions, and a bequest for an act that is qurbah only in his own belief is invalid. The third category, however, is straightforward: when the bequest is for an act that is intrinsically good (qurbah haqiqah) and also recognized as meritorious by the dhimmi's religious tradition, the will is valid without any dispute. This passage from the Hidayah commentary gives two classic examples: lighting lamps in Bayt al-Maqdis and fighting against the Byzantines (Romans).

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that this is the third type of dhimmi will: the bequeathed act is qurbah for both Muslims and the dhimmi's own religion.
2
Examine the examples
Note the two examples: lighting lamps in Bayt al-Maqdis (a shared holy site) and fighting against the Turks/Romans (a common enemy). These acts are meritorious in both belief systems.
The 'Turks' in the historical context refer to the Byzantine Romans, not the Central Asian Turks.
3
State the ruling
The bequest is permissible (jaiz) unconditionally. It does not matter whether the beneficiaries are specified or not.
This is unlike the first category where specification was required.
4
Understand the rationale
The reason for validity is that the act is a true qurbah (qurbah haqiqah) and also considered qurbah in the dhimmi's own belief. Hence there is no conflict or uncertainty.
The term 'qurbah haqiqah' signifies intrinsic merit recognized by sound natural disposition, not merely legal convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اذا اوصى بما يكون قربة في حقنا و في حقهم كما اذا اوصى بان يسرح في بيت المقدس او يغزى الترك وهو من الروم وهذا جائز سواء كان القوم باعيانهم أو بغير اعيانهم لانه وصية بما هو قربة حقيقة و في معتقدهم ايضا
And among them is when he bequeaths something which is a religious act in our right and in their right, such as when he bequeaths that lamps be lit in Bayt al-Maqdis (Jerusalem), or that the Turks (i.e., the Romans) be fought. This is permissible whether the people are specific or non-specific, because it is a bequest of something which is a true act of nearness (qurbah haqiqah) and also in their belief.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage describes the third category: the bequest is for an act that both Muslims and dhimmis consider qurbah. Two examples are given: lighting lamps in Bayt al-Maqdis (the Noble Sanctuary in Jerusalem) and fighting against the Turks (the Romans, i.e., the Byzantine/Roman enemies). These acts are meritorious in both religions. The ruling is that such a will is valid (جائز) without any dispute (بالاتفاق), and importantly, it does not matter whether the beneficiaries are specified or not (سواء كان القوم باعيانهم أو بغير اعيانهم). The reason given is that the bequest is for a true act of qurbah (قربة حقيقة) that is recognized by both belief systems. Therefore, the will is executed as intended, regardless of the dhimmi's own faith, because the act itself is objectively good (qurbah haqiqah). This category is straightforward and free from the complications of the first two categories.

Key Terms
قربة حقيقة (qurbah haqiqah): An act that is truly meritorious in itself, recognized by both Islamic and the dhimmi's religious standards.
بيت المقدس (Bayt al-Maqdis): The Holy House in Jerusalem, referring to the Al-Aqsa Mosque compound, a place of religious significance for both Muslims and Christians/Jews.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third category, why is a dhimmi's bequest to fight the Turks (Romans) valid?
A) Because fighting increases the property value.
B) Because it is a qurbah in both Islam and the dhimmi's belief.
C) Because the Turks are not Muslims.
D) Because the beneficiaries are unspecified.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's bequest for an act that is intrinsically meritorious (qurbah haqiqah) and recognized by his own faith is valid unconditionally by Hanafi consensus.

Bequests to Sinful Recipients: The Fourth Category of Dhimmi Wills
Explains the fourth category of dhimmi wills: bequests to sinful acts are invalid unless specific beneficiaries are named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for a dhimmi's bequest to sinful activities and identify the exception that makes it valid.

Core Idea
A bequest to a sinful cause is invalid unless beneficiaries are specific individuals, making it a valid transfer of ownership.

We have seen that wills for qurbah (religious acts) in one or both faiths are valid. Now we turn to the opposite: wills for sinful acts. The Hanafi ruling balances the prohibition of sin with the validity of property transfer when beneficiaries are identified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the bequest
What is being bequeathed? The passage gives examples: bequests to female singers (al-mughaniyat) and wailing women (al-naihat). These are activities considered sinful in both Islam and the dhimmi's faith.
The key is that the object is a sin (ma'siyah) in both traditions.
2
Determine the general ruling
The general ruling is that such a bequest is not permissible (ghayr ja'iz) because it is a sin. The text states 'fain this ghayr ja'iz li'annahu ma'siyah' (this is not permissible because it is disobedience).
The ruling is based on the prohibition of aiding sin.
3
Apply the exception
The exception is when the bequest is made to specific individuals (قوم باعيانهم). In that case, it becomes valid as a tamlik (transfer of ownership) and istikhlaf (bequest). The specificity transforms the will into a gift to identified persons.
The justification is that the will no longer endorses the sinful activity generally but is a property transfer to named persons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها اذا اوصى بما لا يكون قربة لا فى حقنا ولا فى حقهم كما اذا اوصى للمغنيات والنائحات فان هذا غير جائز لانه معصية في حقنا و في حقهم الا ان يكون لقوم باعيانهم فيصح تمليكا و استخلافاً
And among them is when he bequeaths something which is not a religious act either in our right or in their right, such as when he bequeaths to female singers (al-mughaniyat) and wailing women (al-naihat). Then this is not permissible because it is disobedience (ma'siyah) in our right and in their right, unless it is for specific individuals, in which case it is valid as a tamlik (transfer of ownership) and as a bequest (istikhlaf).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage covers the fourth and final category: the bequest is for something that is not qurbah for either Muslims or dhimmis; it is actually a sin (ma'siyah) in both traditions. The examples given are bequests to female singers (مغنيات) and wailing women (نائحات) — activities considered sinful in Islam and likely also in Christian/Jewish morality. The general ruling is that such a will is not permissible (غير جائز) because it is a sin. However, there is an exception: if the bequest is made to a specific group of individuals (قوم باعيانهم), it becomes valid as a transfer of ownership (تمليكا) and as an istikhlaf (a bequest of succession). The reasoning is that since the beneficiaries are identified, the will is essentially a gift of ownership to them, and the sinful nature of the intended use does not invalidate the transfer itself. This parallels the exception in the second category: specifying the beneficiaries overrides the lack of qurbah.

Key Terms
مغنيات (al-mughaniyat): Female singers, specifically those who sing for entertainment in a manner considered sinful.
نائحات (al-naihat): Wailing women, who lament loudly at funerals, considered a sinful practice in Islam.
معصية (ma'siyah): Disobedience to God; a sinful act.
استخلاف (istikhlaf): A bequest that serves as a succession or appointment, here used synonymously with tamlik? Or a specific type of will?

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth category, when does a dhimmi's bequest to female singers become valid?
A) When the money is used for charitable purposes.
B) When the bequest is made to a specific group of individuals.
C) When the dhimmi converts to Islam.
D) When the singers are related to the dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's bequest to a sinful cause is invalid generally, but valid if the beneficiaries are identified, because it becomes a transfer of ownership.

Will of a Bid'ati: Two Cases
Ruling on the will of a bid'ati depending on whether his innovation reaches kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a bid'ati's will in both cases (innovation reaching kufr or not) and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the apostate's transactions.

Core Idea
A bid'ati's will is valid unless his innovation constitutes kufr, where the dispute on apostate's transactions applies.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition addresses the validity of a will made by a person with heretical innovations (bid'ah). The ruling hinges on whether the innovation reaches the level of disbelief (kufr). Hanafi law judges by external appearances, so unless the bid'ah is clearly kufr, the person retains Muslim status in legal transactions. If it is kufr, the person is treated as an apostate, and a known dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions arises regarding the validity of his transactions, including his will.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of bid'ati
Determine whether the innovation (bid'ah) leads to disbelief (kufr) or not. The passage gives two separate rulings based on this distinction.
Hanafi fiqh does not automatically label all bid'ah as kufr; only if the belief contradicts a necessary tenet of Islam.
2
Apply the ruling for non-kufr bid'ah
If the bid'ah does not constitute kufr, the person is treated like a Muslim in matters of wills. The will is valid, subject to the usual limits (one-third of estate).
The principle: we judge by the apparent (ẓāhir) unless evidence of apostasy exists.
3
Apply the ruling for kufr-level bid'ah
If the bid'ah reaches kufr, the person is treated as an apostate (murtad). For a male apostate, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa suspends his transactions, while the Ṣāḥibayn deem them valid.
For a female apostate, all agree her will is valid because she remains in apostasy indefinitely.
4
Locate the authoritative position
Identify the muftā bihī (operative ruling) from the dispute. Later Hanafi fatwa practice prefers the Ṣāḥibayn’s view: the apostate's will is valid.
This is supported by the principle of ease and the preservation of contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و صاحب الهوى ان كان لا يكفر فهو فى حق الوصية بمنزلة المسلمين لأنا امرنا ببناء الاحكام على الظاهر وان كان يكفر فهو بمنزلة المرتد فيكون على الخلاف المعروف فى تصرفاته بين ابي حنيفة و صاحبيه و فى المرتدة الاصح انه تصح وصاياها لانها تبقى على الردة بخلاف المرتد لانه يقتل او يسلم
And the person of innovation: if he is not declared an unbeliever, then in the matter of will he is in the position of Muslims, because we have been commanded to base rulings on the apparent. And if he is declared an unbeliever, then he is in the position of an apostate, and thus it is according to the well-known disagreement regarding his transactions between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. Regarding the female apostate, the most correct is that her wills are valid because she remains in apostasy, unlike the male apostate because he is killed or becomes Muslim.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on the will of a person who holds innovative beliefs (bid'ah). The author distinguishes two cases. First, if the innovation does not reach the level of kufr (disbelief), the person is treated as a Muslim in matters of wills. We are commanded to judge by external appearances (zāhir), so we apply the same rulings unless clear evidence of apostasy exists. Second, if the innovation does reach kufr, the person is treated as an apostate (murtad). For a male apostate, there is a well-known difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): Abu Hanifa holds that his transactions, including his will, are suspended (mawqūf) until he either returns to Islam or is executed. The Sahibayn hold that his transactions are valid (jā'iz). For a female apostate (murtaddah), the most authentic position (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that her will is valid because she is not executed; she remains in apostasy indefinitely until she repents. Therefore, her situation is like that of a dhimmī, and her transactions are valid. The key principle is that the limitation of one-third (thuluth) for wills is tied to the rights of heirs, and for a bid'ati who is not an apostate, the normal rules for Muslim testators apply.

Key Terms
صاحب الهوى (ṣāḥib al-hawā): A person who holds heretical or innovated beliefs (bid'ah) that may or may not reach the level of disbelief.
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate from Islam, a person who leaves the religion.
وصية (waṣiyyah): A bequest or will made by a person to transfer ownership of property after death to someone other than an heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for treating a non-kufr bid'ati as a Muslim regarding wills?
A) The bid'ati is actually a Muslim inwardly.
B) We are commanded to base rulings on the apparent (zāhir).
C) The bid'ati's repentance is accepted.
D) His will is limited to one-third.

Key Takeaway
A bid'ati's will is valid if his innovation does not reach kufr; if it does, the will is subject to a dispute, but the mufta bihi (Ṣāḥibayn) holds it valid, and a female apostate's will is always valid.

Validity of a Musta'min's Will for His Entire Estate
Ruling on the validity of a musta'min's will giving all his wealth to a Muslim or dhimmi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a harbi musta'min's will for all his wealth and explain the reasoning based on the rights of heirs and the nature of amān.

Core Idea
A musta'min's will is valid for his entire estate because his heirs in Dar al-Harb have no recognized right.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling that may seem to contradict the general limit of one-third for bequests. The passage clarifies that this limit exists to protect the interests of heirs, but when those heirs have no legal standing, the limit is lifted. Understanding this exception deepens your grasp of the rationale behind inheritance and will provisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (A harbi musta'min bequeaths all his wealth to a Muslim or dhimmi). What is the hukm? (Valid/permissible).
2
Locate the proof (dalīl)
What evidence does the author provide? Two rational reasons: (1) the one-third limit is waived because heirs have no right; (2) the amān is personal, not for the heirs.
The proof is rational (dalīl 'aqlī), not textual.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
How does the reasoning lead to the ruling? The prohibition of bequests beyond one-third is linked to the right of heirs. Since these heirs have no recognized right, the prohibition does not apply.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
Do other schools disagree? The ruling is mukhtalaf fih; some schools may require the one-third limit for all testators regardless of heirs' location.
5
Evaluate the tarjīh
Which position is relied upon? The Hanafi position from ẓāhir al-riwāyah is muftā bihi, based on the consistent application of the principle that the limit protects heirs' rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا دخل الحربى دارنا بامان فاوصى لمسلم او ذمي بماله كله جاز لان امتناع الوصية بما زاد على الثلث لحق الورثة ولهذا ينفذ باجازتهم و ليس لورثته حق مرعى لكونهم فى دار الحرب اذهم اموات في حقنا ولان حرمة ماله باعتبار الامان والامان كان لحقه لا لحق ورثته
He said: And when a harbi enters our land with aman and wills to a Muslim or dhimmi all his wealth, it is permissible, because the prohibition of bequeathing more than one-third is due to the right of the heirs, and for this reason it is executed with their permission. And his heirs have no right that is regarded because they are in dar al-harb, as they are dead in our regard, and because the sanctity of his property is on account of the aman, and the aman was for his own right, not for the right of his heirs.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) who enters Dar al-Islam with a guarantee of safety (amān) and then makes a will (waṣiyyah) bequeathing his entire estate to a Muslim or a dhimmi (a protected non-Muslim). The ruling is that this will is valid (jā'iz) and effective for the whole estate, not just one-third. The author provides two reasons. First, the legal restriction that a will cannot exceed one-third of the estate exists to protect the rights of the testator's heirs. In this case, the musta'min's heirs are in Dar al-Harb; from the perspective of Islamic law, they are like dead people—they have no legal standing, and their rights are not recognized. Second, the inviolability (ḥurmah) of the musta'min's property is derived from the amān granted to him personally. The amān does not extend to his heirs; therefore, the heirs have no claim that would necessitate limiting the will. Consequently, the musta'min may dispose of his entire property through a will. This ruling is based on rational reasoning (dalīl 'aqlī) from the principles of amān and the status of harbīs.

Key Terms
حربی (ḥarbī): A non-Muslim who is not under the protection of the Islamic state and is considered an enemy, residing in Dar al-Harb.
مستامن (musta'min): A harbī who enters Dar al-Islam with a temporary guarantee of safety (amān).
امان (amān): A guarantee of safety and protection granted to a non-Muslim from Dar al-Harb entering Dar al-Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the will of a harbi musta'min for all his wealth valid despite the general rule limiting bequests to one-third?
A) Because he is not a Muslim, so the rule does not apply.
B) Because his heirs in Dar al-Harb have no recognized right that would limit the will.
C) Because he has received permission from his heirs.
D) Because the amān grants him full property rights including testamentary freedom.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a musta'min to will his entire estate because the rights of his heirs in Dar al-Harb are not recognized, and the amān only protects his own interests.

Musta'min's Bequest for Less and Slave Manumission at Death
Ruling on musta'min's testamentary acts without one-third limit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a musta'min's bequest for less than his entire estate and explain why slave manumission and tadbir at death are valid without the one-third limit.

Core Idea
Musta'min's bequests and manumissions are not limited by one-third because his heirs in Dar al-Harb have no protected right.

This lesson continues our study of the musta'min's testamentary capacity. While a Muslim testator is normally restricted to one-third of his estate for bequests to non-heirs, the musta'min enjoys broader freedom. The passage explains two specific scenarios: a bequest for less than the entire estate and the freeing or tadbir of a slave at death. Understanding the reasoning behind these rulings deepens our grasp of how the Sharī'ah balances rights based on the individual's legal status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two distinct but related testamentary acts of the musta'min: (1) making a wasiyyah (bequest) for less than the entire estate, and (2) freeing a slave ('itq) or making a slave mudabbar (tadbīr) at the point of death.
Both involve testamentary dispositions that normally are limited to one-third of the estate for a Muslim testator.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For the first scenario: the bequest for less than the entire estate is executed, and the remainder is given to his heirs (in Dar al-Harb). For the second: freeing a slave or tadbīr at death is valid without any consideration of the one-third limit.
The passage uses 'faḍālika ṣaḥīḥ minhu' for the second ruling, meaning it is valid from him.
3
Explain the rationale
The ruling is based on the earlier explanation (mimmā bayannā) that the one-third limit exists to protect the rights of the testator's Muslim heirs. Since the musta'min's heirs in Dar al-Harb have no recognized right over his estate under Hanafi law (due to different jurisdictions), the restriction does not apply. Instead, the musta'min's own right (amān) requires that his estate be delivered to his proper heirs.
This rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) shows that the ruling is derived from the underlying ratio legis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان اوصى باقل من ذالك اخذت الوصية و يرد الباقى على ورثته و ذالك من حق المستامن ايضاً ولو اعتق عبده عند الموت او دبر عبده في دار الاسلام فذالك صحيح منه من غير اعتبار الثلث لما بينا
And if he willed less than that, the bequest is taken, and the remainder is returned to his heirs, and that is also from the right of the musta'min. And if he freed his slave at death or made his slave a mudabbar in Dar al-Islam, then that is valid from him without consideration of the one-third limit, for what we have explained.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on musta'min's testamentary acts. Two additional rulings are given. First, if the musta'min does not will his entire estate but only a portion, that bequest is given, and the remainder of his property is returned to his heirs in Dar al-Harb. However, this return is not because of any right of those heirs; rather, it is because the musta'min's own right (his amān) requires that his estate be delivered to his proper heirs. Second, if the musta'min, at the point of death, either frees a slave ('itq) or makes a slave mudabbar (declares that the slave will be free upon his death), these acts are valid without the usual one-third limitation imposed on Muslim testators. The reason is the same as before: the one-third limit exists to protect the rights of the testator's heirs, and the musta'min's heirs in Dar al-Harb have no such recognized right. Therefore, his manumission and tadbīr are effective from his entire estate. This illustrates that the musta'min enjoys full testamentary capacity regarding his property as long as it does not involve dispositions that require consideration of heirs' rights.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Manumission of a slave; freeing a slave from bondage at the point of death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the death of the master (tadbīr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A musta'min's bequests and slave manumissions at death are not subject to the one-third limit because his heirs in Dar al-Harb have no protected right under Hanafi law.

Will to a Harbi Musta'min is Valid
Ruling that a will by a Muslim or dhimmi to a musta'min is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a bequest from a Muslim or dhimmi to a harbi musta'min and explain the qiyas (analogy) that supports it.

Core Idea
A will to a musta'min is valid because his transactional capacity equals that of a dhimmi while in Dar al-Islam.

This passage clarifies that the legal capacity of a musta'min, despite being an enemy combatant, is equivalent to that of a dhimmi in all transactions (mu'āmalāt) as long as he remains within Dar al-Islam under amān. Consequently, a bequest from a Muslim or dhimmi to such a person is valid, analogous to the validity of bequests to dhimmīs. The ruling is derived through clear qiyās (al-qiyās al-jalī).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Determine who the testator is (a Muslim or a dhimmi) and who the beneficiary is (a harbi musta'min).
2
State the ruling
The author rules that such a bequest is permissible (jā'iz). Memorize: 'لو اوصى له مسلم او ذمى بوصية جاز'.
3
Analyze the proof (dalīl)
The proof is the principle: 'مادام فى دار الاسلام فهو فى المعاملات بمنزلة الذمى' – as long as he is in Dar al-Islam, he is like a dhimmi in transactions.
This is the 'illa (effective cause) for the analogy.
4
Trace the qiyas argument
Apply the qiyas: The asl is a dhimmi receiving a bequest (valid). The far' is a musta'min receiving a bequest. The illa is being in Dar al-Islam with transactional capacity. Hence, the hukm (valid) is transferred.
The author explicitly extends the reasoning: if his lifetime contracts are valid, so are post-death bequests (تبرع).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كذلك لو اوصى له مسلم او ذمى بوصية جاز لانه مادام فى دار الاسلام فهو فى المعاملات بمنزلة الذمى ولهذا تصح عقود التمليكات منه في حال حياته و يصح تبرعه في حياته فكذا بعد مماته
And similarly, if a Muslim or dhimmi made a will in his favor, it is permissible, because as long as he is in Dar al-Islam, he is in transactions like a dhimmi, and for this reason contracts of ownership from him are valid during his life, and his charitable acts are valid during his life, so likewise after his death.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where the beneficiary of a will is a harbi musta'min: a Muslim or dhimmi testator bequeaths something to him. The ruling is that such a bequest is valid (jā'iz). The reasoning is based on qiyās (analogy). While the musta'min is in Dar al-Islam under amān, he is treated like a dhimmi in matters of transactions (mu'āmalāt). A dhimmi has full legal capacity to receive property through contracts of ownership (tamlīk) such as sale, gift, and bequest during his lifetime. Similarly, his charitable grants (tabarru'āt) are valid during his life. Therefore, just as a Muslim can validly make a will in favor of a dhimmi, he can also make a will in favor of a musta'min. The legal capacity to receive a bequest is the same, as the musta'min is considered a legal person capable of owning property and receiving it by bequest while he remains within Dar al-Islam. This ruling is based on the principle that the musta'min enjoys the same transactional capacity as a dhimmī by virtue of the amān.

Key Terms
المعاملات (al-mu'āmalāt): Transactions and dealings, including contracts of sale, gift, bequest, and other exchanges of property.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary charitable act or gift without compensation, including bequests, gifts, and endowments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the musta'min is a temporary resident and has property rights.
B) Because the musta'min is like a dhimmi in transactions while in Dar al-Islam.
C) Because the Muslim testator has given permission.
D) Because the musta'min has converted to Islam.

Key Takeaway
A bequest to a musta'min is valid because his transactional capacity equals that of a dhimmi while he remains in Dar al-Islam.

Bequest to a Harbi Musta'min: Validity and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bequest to harbi musta'min

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequest to a harbi musta'min and explain the proofs for both the relied-upon view and the dissenting view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A bequest to a harbi musta'min is valid because he is treated like a dhimmi while in Dar al-Islam.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced question: can a Muslim make a bequest to a non-Muslim who has entered Islamic territory with temporary safe-conduct? The relied-upon Hanafi position says yes, by analogy to a dhimmi. However, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf dissent, arguing that the musta'min's intent to return severs the legal bond. This lesson unpacks the reasoning on both sides and clarifies the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a bequest (wasiyyah) by a Muslim or dhimmi to a harbi musta'min. The first view says it is permissible (ja'iz).
The musta'min is a non-Muslim with temporary safe-conduct.
2
Understand the proof for the first view
The proof is qiyas (analogy) on bequest to a dhimmi. While in Dar al-Islam, the musta'min is treated like a dhimmi in all dealings, so bequest is valid.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Understand the dissenting view
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say it is invalid because the musta'min intends to return, there is no permanent protection (dhimmah), and the separation of abodes (tabayun al-darayn) severs mutual rights.
The musta'min can only stay up to one year without jizya.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
If valid, the property goes to the musta'min; if invalid, it remains with the heirs.
Practical consequence of the disagreement.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The first view (permissibility) is the operative ruling, as presented first in the matn and attributed to the majority or Imam Muhammad.
The dissenting view is from Nawadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح اگر کوئی مسلمان یا ذمی حربی مستامن کے لئے وصیت کرے تو یہ جائز ہے یا نہیں تو فرمایا کہ جائز ہے اس لئے کہ مسلمان اگر ذمی کے لئے وصیت کرے تو جائز ہے اور حربی مستامن جب تک کہ وہ ہمارے دار الاسلام میں ہے تمام معاملات میں ذمی کے درجہ میں ہے لہذا حربی مستامن کے لئے بھی وصیت جائز ہوگی۔ اگر مسلمان اپنی زندگی میں حربی مستامن کے ہاتھ بیع وغیرہ کے معاملات کرے تو جائز ہے جیسے ذمی کے ہاتھ جائز ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر مسلمان اپنی زندگی میں حربی مستامن کے لئے کوئی متبرع کرے ہبہ وغیرہ کرے تو جائز ہے۔ تو اسی طرح اگر مرنے کے بعد مسلمان حربی مستامن پر کوئی تبرع کرے تو وہ بھی جائز ہوگا۔ شیخین کا نقطہ نظر وعن ابي حنيفة و ابي يوسف انه لا يجوز لانه مستامن من اهل الحرب اذ هو على قصد الرجوع و يمكن منه ولا يمكن من زيادة المقام على السنة الا بالجزية ترجمہ اور ابوحنیفہ اور ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ یہ جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ وہ ایسا مستامن ہے جو اہل حرب میں سے ہے اس لئے کہ وہ رجوع کے ارادہ پر ہے اور اس کو رجوع کی قدرت دی جائے گی اور ایک سال سے زیادہ پھہرنے کی اجازت نہیں دی جائے گی مگر جزیہ کے ساتھ۔ تشریح ... اگر مسلمان ذمی کو وصیت کرے تو جائز ہے لیکن حضرات شیخین سے یہ بھی منقول ہے کہ یہ جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ مستامن حربی ہے اور دار الاسلام اور دارالحرب کے درمیان تبائین کی وجہ سے آپس میں عصمت و موالات منقطع ہے لہذا اس پر یہ احسان کرنا باطل ہو گا۔ اور وہ حربی اس لئے ہے کہ اس کا ارادہ واپس چلے جانے کا ہے اور اگر وہ واپس جانا چاہے تو اس کو روکا نہیں جائے گا ہاں البتہ اگر یہاں ٹھہر نا چاہے تو ایک سال سے زیادہ نہیں ٹھہر سکتا اور اگر ایک سال سے زیادہ ٹھہر گیا تو اب اس پر تجزیہ واجب کر دیا جائے گا اور اب دار الحرب واپس جانے کی قدرت نہیں دی جائے گی بلکہ وہ ذمی کے درجہ میں ہو جائے گا۔
Explanation: If a Muslim or a dhimmi makes a bequest to a harbi musta'min (one with safe-conduct), it is said it is permissible because if a Muslim makes a bequest to a dhimmi it is permissible, and the harbi musta'min, as long as he is in our Dar al-Islam, is in the rank of a dhimmi in all dealings. Therefore bequest to a harbi musta'min is also permissible. If a Muslim carries out transactions like sale with a harbi musta'min in his lifetime, it is permissible just as with a dhimmi. Similarly, if a Muslim makes a gratuitous transfer (hiba, etc.) to a harbi musta'min during his lifetime, it is permissible. So likewise, after death, if a Muslim makes a gratuitous transfer (bequest) to a harbi musta'min, it will also be permissible. The view of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): It is narrated from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that it is not permissible because he is a musta'min from the people of war, for he intends to return, and he can be enabled (to return), and he cannot be allowed to stay beyond one year except with jizya. Translation: And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is narrated that this is not permissible because he is a musta'min from the people of war, because he intends to return, and he will be given the ability to return, and he will not be allowed to stay beyond one year except with jizya. Explanation: ... If a Muslim makes a bequest to a dhimmi it is permissible, but from the shaykhayn it is narrated that this is not permissible because this musta'min is harbi, and due to the divergence between Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb, the bond of protection and friendship is severed, so doing this favor is void. And he is harbi because his intention is to return, and if he wishes to return, he will not be prevented, and if he wishes to stay, he cannot stay more than one year unless jizya is imposed, and if he stays more than one year, then jizya becomes obligatory upon him and he will no longer be able to return to Dar al-Harb, but will become in the rank of a dhimmi.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on bequest to a harbi musta'min. The relied-upon position is that it is permissible, analogizing the musta'min to a dhimmi while he is in Dar al-Islam. The ruling is based on the principle that the musta'min enjoys similar legal capacity in transactions during his stay. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) differ, arguing that the musta'min is essentially a harbi with the intent to return, so there is no permanent bond of protection (dhimmah). The commentary explains that the musta'min can be forced to leave after one year unless he accepts jizya, making him like a dhimmi only if he stays longer. The tarjih is not explicitly stated, but the first ruling is presented as the primary view.

Key Terms
مستامن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Dar al-Islam with a temporary safe-conduct (aman).
حربی (harbi): A non-Muslim from Dar al-Harb, not under Islamic rule or protection.
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim permanently residing in Dar al-Islam with a covenant of protection (dhimmah).
تباین دارین (tabayun al-darayn): The legal distinction and separation between Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb, which affects inheritance and other inter-personal rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, why is a bequest to a harbi musta'min valid?
A) Because the musta'min intends to become Muslim.
B) Because the musta'min is treated like a dhimmi during his stay.
C) Because the testator can choose any beneficiary.
D) Because the musta'min is a citizen of Dar al-Islam.

Key Takeaway
A bequest to a harbi musta'min is valid in the Hanafi school because he enjoys the same legal capacity as a dhimmi for transactions while in Dar al-Islam.

Dhimmi Bequest: The One-Third Limit and Prohibition of Bequest to Heirs
Ruling on dhimmi's bequest exceeding one-third or to heirs with qiyas to Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a dhimmi cannot bequeath more than one-third of his property or bequeath to an heir, and explain the qiyas to Muslims.

Core Idea
A dhimmi is bound by the same bequest limits as a Muslim: cannot exceed one-third or bequeath to an heir.

This rule establishes that dhimmis, though not required to follow all Islamic laws, must adhere to Islamic transactional rules. Thus, the one-third limitation and prohibition of bequest to heirs apply to them just as to Muslims, based on qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a dhimmi making a bequest exceeding one-third or to an heir. The ruling is it is not permissible (la yajuz).
2
Recognize the qiyas basis
The author compares the dhimmi to a Muslim, stating that since Muslims are restricted, so are dhimmis, because they have undertaken Islamic transactional laws.
The phrase 'اعتبارا بالمسلمين' indicates qiyas.
3
Understand the rationale
The dhimmi's covenant includes accepting Islamic law in transactions. Bequest is a transaction.
The text says 'لانهم التزموا احكام الاسلام فيما يرجع الى المعاملات'.
4
Conclude the ruling
The bequest is invalid, and the wealth is distributed according to inheritance rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أوصى الذمى باكثر من الثلث او لبعض ورثته لا يجوز اعتبارا بالمسلمين لانهم التزموا احكام الاسلام فيما يرجع الى المعاملات ولو اوصى لخلاف ملته جاز اعتباراً بالارث اذ الكفر كله ملة واحدة ولو أوصى لحربى فى دار الاسلام لا يجوز لان الارث ممتنع لتباين الدارين والوصية اخته والله أعلم تشریح مصنف کچھ اور مزید جزئیات پیش فرمارہے ہیں، اگر ذمی نے تہائی مال سے زیادہ کی وصیت کی یا اپنے ورثہ کے لئے وصیت کی تو کیا یہ جائز ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ نہیں ، اس لئے کہ یہ مسلمانوں کے لئے جائز نہیں ذمیوں کے لئے بھی جائز نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وہ احکام جن کا معاملات سے تعلق ہے ان احکام کے اندرابل ذمہ مسلمانوں کے مثل ہیں اور یہ معاملہ ہے جس میں اہل ذمہ مسلمانوں کے مثل ہیں۔ -۲- اگر کسی ذمی یہودی نے نصرانی ذمی کے لئے وصیت کر دی تو کیا حکم ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ جائز ہے کیونکہ کفر ملت واحدہ ہے یعنی تمام کفار ایک کھیت کے بتھوے ہیں ۔ - اور اگر اس ذمی نے جو دارالاسلام میں ہے کسی ایسے حربی کے لئے وصیت کر دی جو دار الحرب میں ہے تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ صورت مذکورہ میں تباین دارین کی وجہ سے ارث بھی منتفع ہے تو وصیت بھی ممتنع ہو گی اس لئے کہ وصیت میراث کی بہن ہے۔
And if a dhimmi makes a bequest of more than one-third or for some of his heirs, it is not permissible, by analogy to Muslims, because they have undertaken the laws of Islam regarding transactions. And if he makes a bequest for someone of a different religion, it is permissible, by analogy to inheritance, because all disbelief is one religion. And if he makes a bequest for a harbi in Dar al-Islam, it is not permissible because inheritance is prevented due to the divergence of the two abodes, and bequest is its sister. And Allah knows best. Explanation: The author presents further details: If a dhimmi makes a bequest of more than one-third of his property or for his heirs, is it permissible? He said no, because it is not permissible for Muslims, so for dhimmis it will also not be permissible, since they are like Muslims in the rules that pertain to transactions, and this is a transactional matter. Second: If a Jewish dhimmi makes a bequest to a Christian dhimmi, what is the ruling? He said it is permissible because disbelief is one religion, meaning all disbelievers are of the same kind. Third: And if a dhimmi who is in Dar al-Islam makes a bequest to a harbi who is in Dar al-Harb, then this is not permissible because in the mentioned case, due to the divergence of the abodes, inheritance is also negated, so bequest will also be prevented, because bequest is the sister of inheritance.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage covers three specific sub-rules regarding a dhimmi's bequests. First, a dhimmi is restricted to one-third of his property for non-heirs and cannot bequeath to an heir, just like a Muslim, because dhimmis are bound by Islamic transactional laws. Second, a dhimmi may bequeath to a dhimmi of a different faith (e.g., a Jew to a Christian) because legally all disbelief constitutes one milla (religious community), analogizing to inheritance where a Jew may inherit from a Christian (in Hanafi law). Third, a dhimmi cannot bequeath to a harbi located in Dar al-Harb because inheritance between the two abodes is impossible due to the divergence of domains, and bequest follows the same principle as inheritance. The reasoning is based on analogy (qiyas) to Muslim rules and to inheritance rules.

Key Terms
ملت (millah): Religious community or creed. Here, 'all disbelief is one millah' means all types of kufr are considered one legal category for purposes of bequest and inheritance.
تباین دارین (tabayun al-darayn): The legal distinction between Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb that prevents inheritance and bequest between them.
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim permanent resident under Islamic protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a bequest from a dhimmi to his heir invalid?
A) Because the heir is a non-Muslim.
B) Because bequest to an heir is prohibited for Muslims and by qiyas for dhimmis.
C) Because the dhimmi is not allowed to make any bequests.
D) Because the bequest always exceeds one-third.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi is bound by the same one-third limit and prohibition of bequest to heirs as a Muslim, due to the analogy based on his undertaking of Islamic transactional laws.

Dhimmi Bequest to a Dhimmi of a Different Faith
Ruling on dhimmi's bequest to a dhimmi of different faith with qiyas to inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a dhimmi may bequeath to a dhimmi of a different faith, and explain the qiyas to inheritance based on the principle that 'all disbelief is one millah'.

Core Idea
Disbelief is one millah: a dhimmi can bequeath to another dhimmi of any faith because they share the same legal category of kufr.

This rule addresses cross-faith bequests among non-Muslims. Unlike the Shafi'i school which treats different non-Muslim faiths as separate, the Hanafi school considers all kufr as one legal category for inheritance and bequest, allowing a Jew to bequeath to a Christian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a dhimmi (e.g., Jewish) making a bequest to another dhimmi of a different faith (e.g., Christian). The ruling is it is permissible (jā'iz).
2
Note the qiyas to inheritance
The author analogizes bequest to inheritance. In Hanafi law, a Jew can inherit from a Christian because all disbelief is considered one religious community.
The text says 'اعتباراً بالارث'.
3
Understand the principle
The principle is 'الكفر كله ملة واحدة' – all disbelief is one millah. This means that for purposes of inheritance and bequest, all non-Muslims are treated as a single category.
4
Apply the ruling
Since the dhimmi is part of the millah of kufr, he may bequeath to any other dhimmi, regardless of specific faith.
The ruling is valid and has no restriction of faith difference.

Dhimmi Bequest to a Harbi in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on dhimmi's bequest to a harbi in Dar al-Harb with qiyas to inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a dhimmi cannot bequeath to a harbi in Dar al-Harb, and explain the qiyas to inheritance due to tabayun al-darayn.

Core Idea
Separation of abodes prevents inheritance, and bequest follows the same rule: a dhimmi cannot bequeath to a harbi in Dar al-Harb.

This rule addresses bequests between a dhimmi in Dar al-Islam and a harbi in Dar al-Harb. Since inheritance between the two abodes is impossible due to tabayun al-darayn, bequest is also invalid because 'bequest is the sister of inheritance'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a dhimmi in Dar al-Islam making a bequest to a harbi in Dar al-Harb. The ruling is it is not permissible (la yajuz).
2
Note the qiyas to inheritance
The author states that inheritance is prevented (mumtani') between the two abodes due to tabayun al-darayn. Bequest follows the same rule because it is 'the sister of inheritance'.
The phrase 'والوصية اخته' indicates that bequest and inheritance share the same legal principles.
3
Understand the rationale
The divergence of abodes creates a complete legal separation. A harbi is not a subject of Islamic law, and there is no mutual inheritance rights.
4
Apply the ruling
Since inheritance is impossible, bequest is also invalid. The wealth cannot be transferred across the abodes.
This ruling is consistent with the principle that bequest is a branch of inheritance.

The Binding Nature of a Wasi's Acceptance
Ruling on validity of a wasi's rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a wasi's rejection after acceptance in the testator's presence is invalid and distinguish it from the case of a wakil.

Core Idea
A wasi's acceptance is binding; rejecting later would deceive the testator.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from the chapter on executorship (wasiyyah). The classical Hanafi text from al-Quduri states that once a person accepts the role of executor (wasi) in the presence of the testator, any later rejection in the testator's absence is invalid. The reasoning rests on protecting the testator's reliance. We also compare this with the case of an ordinary agent (wakil), who can validly reject in the principal's absence because no similar harm occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the passage: a person accepts executorship in the testator's presence, then rejects it in his absence. The ruling is that the rejection is not valid (laysa bi-radd).
The initial acceptance creates a binding obligation.
2
Locate the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The text gives the reason: the deceased (testator) proceeded on his path relying on the wasi. Allowing rejection would cause deception (maghrur).
The harm (darar) to the testator is the key consideration.
3
Distinguish from the wakil case
The author contrasts a wakil (agent) appointed to buy/sell. In the agent's case, rejection in the principal's absence is valid because the principal is alive and can act himself, so no harm occurs.
The difference hinges on the ability of the principal to act in his own behalf.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Imagine Zaid appoints Bakr as wasi in his will. Bakr accepts in Zaid's presence. Later, after Zaid dies, Bakr tries to reject. According to this ruling, Bakr's rejection is invalid – he remains bound.
The testator's death eliminates the possibility of his own action, increasing the harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of the Executor and What He Possesses. Translation: This is a chapter about the executor and his powers. Explanation: So far the author has explained the rulings concerning the testator; now he will explain the rulings concerning the nominee (al-musa ilayh), who is called the wasi (executor), who manages the property of the testator after his death. In any case, the rulings of the testator are many and his occurrence is frequent, so the author presented the rulings of the testator first and the rulings of the wasi later. The executor: if he accepts the executorship in the presence of the testator and then rejects it in his absence, the rejection is not valid. The author said: And whoever appoints a man as executor, and the executor accepts in the presence of the testator and rejects it in his absence, it is not a valid rejection, because the deceased has proceeded on his path relying on him; so if his rejection in his absence were valid, during his lifetime or after his death, he would become deceived by him, so his rejection is nullified. This is unlike the case of an agent (wakil) for purchasing an unspecified slave or for selling his property, where his (the agent's) rejection in the absence of the principal is valid, because there is no harm there, since the principal is alive and able to act himself.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This lesson discusses the appointment of an executor (wasi). The key ruling: if a person appoints someone as wasi, and the wasi accepts in the testator's presence, any later rejection in the testator's absence is invalid. The reason is that the testator acts based on that trust, and if the wasi later rejects, it would deceive the testator. This is contrasted with a wakil (agent) who can decline later because the principal is alive and can act himself. The ruling protects the testator's reliance. The source is a quote from al-Quduri.

Key Terms
وصی (wasi): An executor appointed to manage the testator's estate after death.
وکیل (wakil): An agent appointed to act on behalf of a living principal.
رد (radd): Rejection of an appointment (executorship or agency).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the testator is no longer alive to appoint someone else.
B) Because the testator relied on that acceptance, and allowing rejection would cause deception.
C) Because the wasi is required by law to accept.
D) Because the wasi is a relative.

Key Takeaway
A wasi's acceptance in the testator's presence is binding; any subsequent rejection is invalid to prevent deception.

Rulings on Wills of Dhimmis: Three Key Cases
Three rulings on wills of dhimmis with legal reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings on wills made by dhimmis and explain the legal principles (dala'il) that justify each ruling.

Core Idea
Dhimmis follow Muslim transactional law; all kufr is one millah; will mirrors inheritance.

This lesson examines three distinct rulings on wills (wasiyyah) made by dhimmis, as presented in Al-Hidayah. The rulings are derived through analogical reasoning based on core Hanafi principles: that dhimmis are bound by the same transactional laws as Muslims, that all forms of disbelief constitute a single legal community (millah wahidah), and that the rules governing wills parallel those of inheritance. Understanding these principles helps you apply the rulings consistently to new scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three distinct situations involving dhimmi wills. For each, note the parties involved and the nature of the bequest.
The three scenarios are: (1) a dhimmi exceeds one-third or bequeaths to an heir, (2) a dhimmi Jew bequeaths to a dhimmi Christian, (3) a dhimmi in Dar al-Islam bequeaths to a harbi in Dar al-Harb.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
From the text, determine whether each will is valid (ja'iz) or invalid (ghayr ja'iz). Pay attention to the explicit 'No' and 'Yes'.
Rulings: (1) invalid, (2) valid, (3) invalid.
3
Identify the legal principle (dalil) for each
The text provides a reason for each ruling. Extract the principle: for (1) 'dhimmis are like Muslims in transactions', for (2) 'kufr is one millah', for (3) 'difference of abodes negates inheritance, and will is the sister of inheritance'.
These are analogical proofs (qiyas) rooted in established usul rules.
4
Apply the principle to explain the ruling
For each scenario, show how the given principle leads to the ruling. This demonstrates the chain of reasoning from principle to particular case.
E.g., (2): Since all kufr is one millah, differences in religion among dhimmis do not prevent a valid will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر ذمی نے تہائی مال سے زیادہ کی وصیت کی یا اپنے ورثہ کے لئے وصیت کی تو کیا یہ جائز ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ نہیں ، اس لئے کہ یہ مسلمانوں کے لئے جائز نہیں ذمیوں کے لئے بھی جائز نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وہ احکام جن کا معاملات سے تعلق ہے ان احکام کے اندرابل ذمہ مسلمانوں کے مثل ہیں اور یہ معاملہ ہے جس میں اہل ذمہ مسلمانوں کے مثل ہیں۔ -۲- اگر کسی ذمی یہودی نے نصرانی ذمی کے لئے وصیت کر دی تو کیا حکم ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ جائز ہے کیونکہ کفر ملت واحدہ ہے یعنی تمام کفار ایک کھیت کے بتھوے ہیں ۔ - اور اگر اس ذمی نے جو دارالاسلام میں ہے کسی ایسے حربی کے لئے وصیت کر دی جو دار الحرب میں ہے تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ صورت مذکورہ میں تباین دارین کی وجہ سے ارث بھی منتفع ہے تو وصیت بھی ممتنع ہو گی اس لئے کہ وصیت میراث کی بہن ہے۔
If a dhimmi makes a will for more than one-third of his property or makes a will for his heirs, is it permissible? He said: No, because it is not permissible for Muslims, so it will not be permissible for dhimmis either, since the rulings that pertain to transactions are the same for dhimmis as for Muslims, and this is a transaction in which dhimmis are like Muslims. 2. If a dhimmi Jew makes a will for a dhimmi Christian, what is the ruling? He said: It is permissible because kufr is one millah (religious community), i.e., all disbelievers are like plants in one field. 3. And if a dhimmi who is in Dar al-Islam makes a will for a harbi who is in Dar al-Harb, it is not permissible because in the mentioned situation, due to the difference of the two abodes, inheritance is also negated, so the will will also be prevented, because the will is the sister of inheritance.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings regarding wills (wasiyyah) made by dhimmis (non-Muslim citizens of the Islamic state). The first ruling: a dhimmi's will exceeding one-third of his property or made in favor of his legal heirs is invalid. This is because dhimmis are governed by the same transactional laws as Muslims; since such wills are invalid for Muslims, they are likewise invalid for dhimmis. The second ruling: a dhimmi Jew may validly bequeath to a dhimmi Christian. The Hanafi principle here is that all forms of disbelief (kufr) constitute a single legal community (millah wahidah) for the purposes of inheritance and bequest, so religious differences within non-Muslims do not prevent validity. The third ruling: a dhimmi's will to a harbi (non-Muslim enemy) residing in Dar al-Harb is invalid. The reason is that inheritance between Muslims and harbis is negated due to the difference of abodes (ikhtilaf al-darayn); since will is considered the 'sister of inheritance' (meaning it follows the same rules), it is likewise invalid.

Key Terms
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under the protection of an Islamic state.
حربی (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Islamic rule and is considered an enemy.
ملت واحدہ (millah wahidah): A single religious community; Hanafis consider all forms of kufr as one legal category for inheritance and bequest.
وصیت (wasiyyah): A will or bequest made by a person regarding disposal of his property after death.
میراث (mirath): Inheritance; the transfer of property from a deceased to his heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) All forms of disbelief constitute a single legal community
C) The difference of abodes allows it
D) The will is the sister of inheritance

Key Takeaway
Dhimmis are bound by the same transactional laws as Muslims regarding wills; all non-Muslims are one legal community (millah wahidah) for bequests; and a will is invalid to a harbi because inheritance is negated across differing abodes.

Executor's Acceptance in Presence and Rejection in Absence
Ruling that executor's rejection in testator's absence is invalid with comparison to agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between executorship (wisayah) and agency (wakalah) regarding the validity of rejection after acceptance, and apply the ruling based on harm (darar).

Core Idea
Once an executor accepts in the testator's presence, rejection after his death is invalid due to harm and deception.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies a critical distinction between executorship and agency. The executor, having accepted the trust in the testator's presence, cannot subsequently revoke it because the testator has died relying on that acceptance. The underlying rationale is the prevention of harm (darar) and deception (ghurur), which are prohibited in Islamic law. In contrast, an agent (wakil) can reject his agency in the principal's absence because the principal is alive and capable of acting himself. We will dissect the ruling, its proof, and its application through a qiyas comparison.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the specific ruling: If the testator (musi) appoints an executor (wasi) and the executor accepts in the testator's presence, then rejects in his absence (during the testator's life or after death), the rejection is invalid (la yasihh). Identify the act (rejection) and the legal consequence (invalid).
The ruling applies only after acceptance in the testator's presence.
2
Extract the 'illa (effective cause)
The text gives the reason: the testator died relying on the executor's acceptance. Allowing rejection would cause harm (darar) and deception (ghurur) to the testator. This harm is the 'illa that prohibits the rejection.
Harm is a central legal principle in Hanafi fiqh: al-darar yuzal.
3
Compare with the analogous case of agency
Understand the qiyas: The text contrasts with an agent (wakil) appointed to buy an unspecified slave or sell property. In agency, rejection in the principal's absence is valid because there is no harm: the principal is alive and can act himself. This shows that the absence of harm makes rejection permissible.
The asl (original case) is agency; the far' (new case) is executorship; the 'illa is presence/absence of harm.
4
Note the exception for specific agency
The passage includes a subtle nuance: If the agency is for a specific slave, and the agent rejects in absence then buys it for himself, the rejection is invalid and the slave belongs to the principal. This exception aligns with the executorship rule because the principal's reliance is similar.
This highlights that the key factor is the degree of reliance and potential for deception.

باب الوصى وما يملكه

قال و من اوصى الى رجل فقبل الوصى فى وجه الموصى وردها فی غیر و جهه فلیس برد لان الميت مضى السيله معتمداً عليه فلوصح زده فى غير وجهه فى حياته او بعد مماته صار مغرورا من جهته فرد رده بخلاف الوكيل بشراء عبد بغير عينه أو ببيع ماله حيث يصح رده فى غير وجهه لانه لاضرر هناك لانه حی قادر على التصرف بنفسه

ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے کسی شخص کو وصی بنایا پس وصی نے وصایت کو موضی کے سامنے قبول کر لیا اور وصی نے موصی کی عدم موجودگی میں وصایت کو رد کر دیا تو رو نہیں ہوا اس لئے کہ میت اپنی راہ پر لگ گیا اسی پر اعتماد کرتے ہوئے پس اگر موصی کی عدم موجودگی میں اس کا رد صیح ہو جائے موچی کی زندگی میں یا اس کے مرنے کے بعد تو وہ وصی کی جانب سے دھو کہ خوردہ ہو جائے گا تو اس کا رو رد کر دیا جائے گا بخلاف اس کے جو کسی غیر معین غلام کو خریدنے کا وکیل ہو یا اس کے مال کو بیچنے کا وکیل ہو اس حیثیت سے کہ موکل کی عدم موجودگی میں وکیل کار د صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ یہاں کوئی ضرر نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ موکل زندہ ہے بذات خود تصرف کرنے پر قادر ہے۔

تشریح زید نے اپنی موت کے قریب خالد کو اپنا دھی بنایا اور خالد نے اس ذمہ داری کو قبول کر لیا یعنی موصی کے سامنے خالد نے قبول کر لیا اور پھر اس کی عدم موجودگی میں وصایت کو رد کر دیا تو وصایت رد نہ ہوگی اس لئے کہ زید تو خالد پر اعتماد کرتے ہوئے دنیا سے رخصت ہو چکا ہے تو اگر خالد کا رد معتبر ہو جائے تو اس میں ضر رشدید ہے اور اس میں زید کو دھوکہ دینا ہے اور دھوکہ دینا حرام ہے لہذا زید کا یہ رو مردود ہوگا۔

ہاں اگر زید نے خالد کو وکیل بنایا کہ میرے لئے کوئی غلام خرید و یا وکیل بنایا کہ میرا مال فروخت کر دو اور خالد نے زید کی عدم موجودگی میں وکالت کا انکار کر دیا تو جائز ہے اس لئے کہ یہاں کوئی ضرر نہیں کیونکہ زید خود زندہ ہے صاحب قدرت ہے اور اس کام کو خود انجام دے سکتا ہے۔

البتہ اگر زید نے کسی معین غلام کو خریدنے کے لئے خالد کو وکیل بنایا تھا اور خالد نے زید کی عدم موجودگی میں وکالت کو رد کر دیا اور پھر اس غلام کو اپنے لئے خرید لیا تو وکالت رد نہ ہوگی اور یہ غلام زید کے لئے ہوگا۔
Chapter of the Executor and His Powers. He (al-Quduri) said: And whoever appoints a man as executor, and the executor accepts in the presence of the testator, and then rejects it in his absence, it is not a valid rejection, because the deceased went on his way relying on him. If his rejection were valid in his absence during his life or after his death, he would have been deceived by him, so his rejection is rejected. Unlike the agent (wakil) for purchasing an unspecified slave or selling his property, where his rejection in absence is valid, because there is no harm there, since the principal is alive and able to act himself. Commentary: Zaid appointed Khalid as his executor near death, and Khalid accepted in Zaid's presence, then rejected in his absence; the rejection is invalid because Zaid departed relying on Khalid. If Khalid's rejection were valid, there would be severe harm and deception, and deception is haram. However, if Zaid appointed Khalid as agent to buy a slave or sell his property, and Khalid rejected in Zaid's absence, it is valid because there is no harm since Zaid is alive and capable. But if Zaid appointed Khalid to buy a specific slave, and Khalid rejected in absence then bought it for himself, the rejection is invalid and the slave belongs to Zaid.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on the executor (al-wasi) and his powers. It first establishes a key distinction between executorship (wisayah) and agency (wakalah). The core ruling: if the testator appoints an executor who accepts in the testator's presence, he cannot subsequently revoke that acceptance in the testator's absence. The reason is that the testator has died relying on the executor's acceptance; allowing revocation would constitute deception (ghurur) and cause harm (darar), which is prohibited. In contrast, an agent (wakil) can validly reject the agency in the principal's absence because the principal is alive and can appoint another or act himself. The passage provides a subtle nuance: if the agency was for the purchase of a specific slave, and the agent rejects and then buys the slave for himself, the rejection is invalid and the slave belongs to the principal. This is because the agency was tied to a specific object, and the principal's reliance was similar to that in executorship. Thus, the general principle is that once the executor has accepted, he is bound; rejection after the testator's reliance is ineffective.

Key Terms
وصی (wasi): Executor appointed to manage the testator's estate after death.
وکیل (wakil): Agent appointed to act on behalf of a living principal.
وصایت (wisayah): The office or function of an executor.
ضرر (darar): Harm or damage; a legal basis for prohibiting an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the executor has no right to reject
B) Because the testator has died relying on the acceptance, causing harm
C) Because the executor is required to fulfill a religious duty
D) Because the testator can still act himself

Key Takeaway
An executor who accepts in the testator's presence is bound; rejection after the testator's reliance is invalid to prevent harm and deception.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
