Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah matn and its Urdu sharh addresses the ruling when heirs (or some of them) complain to the qadi about the wasi. It is a ruling with reasoning (ta'lil). The passage states that the qadi should not immediately remove the wasi upon the heirs' complaint; he must wait until betrayal (khayanah) becomes evident. The reason is that the wasi derived his authority from the deceased, not from the heirs. The deceased appointed the wasi based on trust, and that trust remains until proven broken. If betrayal is proven, the qadi removes the wasi because the basis of his appointment (trustworthiness) has vanished. The qadi then stands in the place of the deceased, as if the deceased had removed the wasi himself. The commentary (Tashrih) elaborates: mere complaint by the heirs is insufficient; only proven betrayal justifies removal. This protects the wasi's right and upholds the deceased's choice. The qadi's role is to ensure the estate is managed by a trustworthy person, and if the wasi proves untrustworthy, the qadi replaces him. This ruling shows the Hanafi emphasis on preserving the deceased's wishes and the stability of executorship.

Key Terms
ورثة (wuratha'): The heirs of the deceased who have a right to the estate.
خیانت (khiyānah): Betrayal of trust, such as misappropriation of estate assets.
ولایت (wilāyah): Authority or guardianship over the estate granted by the deceased to the wasi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A qadi may only remove a wasi upon proven betrayal (khayanah), not on mere complaint, because the wasi's authority stems from the deceased's trust.

Independent Authority of Multiple Executors: Abu Yusuf vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf vs. Sahibayn on independent vs. joint authority of multiple wasis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn regarding independent authority of multiple executors, explain the qiyas and tafriq arguments, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The authority of multiple executors is joint by default unless the testator explicitly grants independence.

This passage dives into an important intra-Hanafi dispute: when a testator appoints two executors, does each have independent authority to act alone, or must they act jointly? Imam Abu Yusuf argues that wilaya (guardianship) is indivisible, so each executor obtains full authority by analogy to marriage guardianship. The Sahibayn (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad) counter that wilaya here arises from delegation (tafwid), which implies joint action, and they distinguish the marriage analogy. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to follow Hanafi qiyas and tafriq reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Yusuf's position and his three proofs
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that each executor has full independent authority. He presents three proofs: (1) wilaya is indivisible, analogizing to two brothers each having complete marriage guardianship over their sister; (2) wasiyya is succession (khilafa), so the successor inherits the testator's complete authority; (3) the testator's selection indicates equal affection, which is like kinship giving full wilaya.
These proofs rely on the indivisible nature of guardianship and the principle of succession.
2
State the Sahibayn's opposing position and their delegation proof
The Sahibayn argue that executors must act jointly. Their proof: guardianship here is by delegation (tafwid); the testator delegated to two, so the condition of togetherness must be honoured – he was satisfied with the dual, not the singular.
They reject the notion that wilaya is always indivisible; delegation determines its scope.
3
Analyse the Sahibayn's rebuttal of Abu Yusuf's qiyas to marriage
The Sahibayn refute the marriage analogy by distinguishing the cause (sabab): marriage guardianship is based on kinship, which is complete in each brother, while executorship is based on the testator's delegation, which is conditional. Furthermore, marriage guardianship imposes a duty (on the guardian) while executorship is merely a permission; one brother fulfills a right when marrying, but one executor usurps the other's right when acting alone.
This is a classic case of tafriq (differentiation) in Hanafi usul.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, one executor can validly sell property or manage affairs without the other's consent. Under the Sahibayn's view, such unilateral acts are invalid unless the testator explicitly allowed independence. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Sahibayn's position: joint action is required by default.
The text does not explicitly state tarjih, but standard Hanafi fatwa follows joint action.

Source Text

كتاب الوصايا
بوصف الكمال ولان اختيار الاب اياهما يوذن باختصاص كل واحد منهما بالشفقة فينزل ذالك منزلة قرابة كل واحد
ترجمہ اس لئے کہ وصایت اس کا طریقہ ولایت ہے اور ولایت ایک وصف شرعی ہے جو غیر متجری ہے پس یہ ولایت ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کیلئے کامل ثابت ہوگی جیسے دو بھائیوں کے لئے ولایت انکاح اور یہ اس لئے کہ وصایت خلافت ہے اور خلافت متحقق ہوتی ہے جب کہ بھی کی جانب ولایت اس طریقہ پر منتقل ہو جیسے موصی کے لئے ثابت تھی اور موصی کے لئے یہ ولایت کمال کے وصف کے ساتھ تھی اور اس لئے کہ باپ (موصی) کا ان دونوں کو اختیار کرنا خبر دیتا ہے ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے شفقت کے ساتھ مختص ہونے کی تو اس اختصاص کو ہر ایک کی قرابت کے درجہ میں اتار لیا جائے گا۔ تشریح یہ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ وصایت کا ما حصل ولایت ہے یعنی موصی نے وصی کو ولایت کا شرف دیا ہے اور ولایت ایک وصف شرعی ہے جو تجزی اور انقسام کو قبول نہیں کرتا ہے تو جب ولایت تجزی کو قبول ہی نہیں کرتی تو ہر وصی کو پوری پوری ولایت حاصل ہوگی اور جب پوری ولایت حاصل ہوگی تو ہر ایک کو دوسرے کے بغیر تصرفات کا حق ہوگا۔ جیسے ایک عورت ہے اور اس کے دو بھائی ہیں تو دونوں کو اس کا نکاح کرنے کی پوری پوری ولایت ہے لہذا ایک کو حق ہے کہ وہ دوسرے کے بغیر بہن کا نکاح کر دے، تو اسی طرح یہاں بھی یہی حق ہوگا۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دوسری دلیل امام ابو یوسف دوسری دلیل دیتے ہوئے فرماتے ہیں کہ وصایت خلافت ہے اور خلافت میں خلیفہ کو ذوات حاصل ہوتی ہے جو اصل کو حاصل ہوتی ہے، تو موجبھی کی ولایت منتقل ہو کر ہر وصی کو ملے گی اور موسی کو یہ ولایت علی سبیل الکمال حاصل تھی تو اسی طرح ہر وصی کو بھی علی سبیل الکمال یہ ولایت ملے گی۔
تیسری دلیل دیتے ہوئے امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ جب زید نے خالد اور بکر دونوں کو بھی بنایا ہے تو اسکا مطلب یہ ہے کہ زید نے ان دونوں کو شفقت کے سلسلہ میں مخصوص مانا ہے تو ان دونوں کا یہ اختصاص بالشفقہ کو یہ درجہ دیا جائے گا کہ ان دونوں کو موصی سے قرابت ہے اور قرابت کیوجہ سے جو ولایت ملتی ہے وہ کامل ہوتی ہے لہذا یہ بھی علی سبیل الکمال ہوگی۔
طرفین کی دلیل
ولهما ان الولاية تثبت بالتفويض فيراعى وصف التفويض وهو وصف الاجتماع اذ هو شرط مفيد وما رضى الموصى الا بالمثنى وليس الواحد كالمثنى
ترجمہ اور طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ولایت تفویض سے ثابت ہوتی ہے تو تفویض کے وصف کی رعایت کی جائے گی اور وصف آنو این وصف اجتماع ہے اس لئے کہ اجتماع شرط مفید ہے اور موصی راضی نہیں ہوا مگر دو سے اور ایک دو کے مثل نہیں ہے۔ تشریح یہ حضرات طرفین کی دلیل ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ وصی کو جو ولایت ملی ہے یہ موصی کی تفویض کی وجہ سے ملی ہے تو تفویض جیسے ہوئی ہے اس کا اعتبار کر لیا جائے گا۔ اور جب موصی نے ولایت کی تفویض کی تو وصف اجتماع کے ساتھ کی ہے لہذا وصف اجتماع کی رعایت کی جائے گی کیونکہ یہ شرط مفید
ہے کیونکہ انفراد کو وہ قوت حاصل نہیں ہوتی جو اجتماع کو ہوا کرتی ہے اور یہاں موصی رو کی رائے سے راضی ہوا ہے نہ کہ ایک کی رائے سے اور ایک دو کے مثل نہیں ہوتا۔
طرفین کی جانب سے امام ابو یوسف کے قیاس کا جواب
بخلاف الاخوين فى الانكاح لان السبب هنالك القرابة و قد قامت بكل منهما كملاً
ترجمہ بخلاف دو بھائیوں کے نکاح کرنے میں اس لئے کہ سب وہاں قرابت ہے اور قرابت ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے ساتھ کامل طریقہ پر قائم ہے۔ تشریح یہ طرفین کی جانب سے امام ابو یوسف کے قیاس کا جواب ہے کیونکہ انھوں نے اس کو ولایت نکاح پر قیاس کیا تھا ( کمامر ). تو حضرات طرفین نے فرمایا کہ اس کو ولایت انکاح پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے اس لئے کہ ولایت انکاح کا سبب قرابت ہے اور قرابت دونوں کے ساتھ برابر قائم ہے جس میں کوئی تجزمی نہیں ہے۔
جواب کی مزید تشریح
ولان الانكاح حق مستحق لها على الولى حتى لو طالبته بانكاحها من كفو يخطبها يجب عليه وههنا حق التصرف للوصى ولهذا يبقى مخيرا في التصرف ففى الاول او فى حقا على صاحبه فصح و في الثاني استوفى لان ولاية التصرف لهما فاذا تصرف وحده حقا لصاحبه فلا يصح
ترجمہ اور اس لئے کہ نکاح کرنا ایک ایسا حق ہے جو واجب ہے عورت کے لئے ولی کے اوپر یہاں تک کہ اگر عورت ولی سے مطالبہ کرے اپنے نکاح کرنے کا اس کفو سے جس نے اس کو نکاح کا پیغام دیا ہے تو یہ نکاح کرنا ولی پر واجب ہے اور یہاں وصی کے لئے تصرف کا حق ہے اس کی وجہ سے وصی تصرف میں مختار ہے تو پہلی صورت میں ولی نے اس حق کو پورا کیا ہے جو اس کے ساتھی پر واجب ہے تو یہ تو صحیح ہے اور دوسری صورت میں وصی نے وصول کیا ہے اس لئے کہ تصرف کی ولایت ان دونوں کو ہے پس جب ایک نے اپنے ساتھی کے حق میں تصرف کیا تو یہ میچ نہیں ہے۔
تشریح طرفین نے امام ابو یوسف کے قیاس کا جو جواب دیا ہے یہ اس کی مزید تشریح ہے۔ فرماتے ہیں کہ ان دونوں میں بہت فرق ہے ولی پر عورت کا نکاح کرنا ایک حق واجبی ہے لہذا اگر کسی کفو نے عورت کو نکاح کا پیغام دیا اور عورت نے اپنے ولی سے نکاح کرنے کا مطالبہ کیا تو ولی پر واجب ہے کہ اس کفو سے اس کا نکاح کرے اور یہاں وصی پر کوئی حق واجبی نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کو یہ ایک تصرف کا اختیار ملا ہے یہی تو وجہ ہے کہ وصی کو تصرف کرنے کا اختیار ہے اس پر حق واجبی نہیں ہے تو نکاح والی صورت میں جب ایک بھائی نے نکاح کر دیا تو یہ جائز ہے کیونکہ اس کے دوسرے بھائی پر جو حق واجبی تھا یہ اس کی ادائیگی ہے۔
اور دوسری صورت میں جب ایک وصی نے تصرف کیا تو یہ دوسرے کے حق واجہی کو ادا کرنا نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کے حق کو چھین لینا ہے تو
Book of Wills. Because of the description of perfection and because the father's selection of both indicates that each is singled out for affection, so that is placed in the position of kinship for each one. Translation: Because will (wasiyya) is a way of guardianship (wilaya), and guardianship is a legal description that is indivisible, so this guardianship will be proven completely for each of them, like guardianship of marriage for two brothers. And this is because will is succession (khilafa), and succession is realized when guardianship is transferred in the same way it was proven for the testator, and for the testator this guardianship was with the description of perfection. And because the father (testator) selecting both informs of his singling out each with affection, so this singling out is placed in the rank of kinship for each. Explanation: This is Imam Abu Yusuf's proof, the gist being that will results in guardianship, i.e., the testator gave the executor the honor of guardianship, and guardianship is a legal description that does not accept division, so when guardianship does not accept division, each executor will have complete guardianship, and when complete guardianship is obtained, each has the right to act without the other. Like a woman with two brothers: both have complete guardianship of marriage, so one has the right to marry her without the other. Similarly here. Imam Abu Yusuf's second proof: will is succession, and in succession, the successor obtains all that the original had, so the testator's guardianship transfers to each executor, and the testator had this guardianship in a perfect manner, so each executor also gets it perfectly. Third proof: when Zayd appointed both Khalid and Bakr, it means he considered both singled out in affection, so this singling out in affection will be given the rank of kinship to the testator, and kinship gives complete guardianship, so this will also be perfect. The proof of the two sides: And for them [the two Imams], guardianship is proven by delegation, so the description of delegation is considered, which is the description of togetherness, as togetherness is a beneficial condition, and the testator was only satisfied with the dual, and one is not like the dual. Translation: The proof of the two sides is that guardianship is proven by delegation, so the description of delegation will be observed, and that description is togetherness, because togetherness is a beneficial condition, and the testator was only satisfied with two, and one is not like two. Explanation: This is the proof of the two Imams. Its gist: the guardianship given to the executor comes from the testator's delegation, so consider the delegation as it occurred. When the testator delegated guardianship with the description of togetherness, so the description of togetherness will be observed, because it is a beneficial condition, because an individual does not have the strength that togetherness has, and here the testator was satisfied with the opinion of two, not one, and one is not like two. Response from the two sides to Imam Abu Yusuf's analogy: Unlike the two brothers in marriage, because the cause there is kinship, and kinship is established completely in each. Translation: Unlike the two brothers in marriage, because the cause there is kinship, and kinship is established completely in each of them. Explanation: This is the response from the two sides to Imam Abu Yusuf's analogy, because he had analogized this to marriage guardianship (as mentioned). The two Imams say that analogizing to marriage guardianship is an analogy with a difference, because marriage guardianship's cause is kinship, and kinship is equally established in both, with no division. Further explanation of the response: And because marriage is a right owed to her from the guardian, such that if she demands that he marry her to a suitor, it is obligatory upon him. But here the right of acting is for the executor, and therefore he remains free to act. So in the first case, the guardian fulfills a right that is owed upon him to his partner, so it is valid. In the second case, the executor takes away, because the guardianship of acting belongs to both, so when one acts alone in the right of his partner, it is not valid. Translation: Because marriage is a right that is obligatory upon the guardian for the woman, to the extent that if she demands her marriage from him with a suitable suitor, it becomes obligatory upon him. But here, the right of acting is for the executor, and therefore he is free in acting. So in the first case, the guardian has fulfilled a right that was obligatory upon his partner, so that is correct. In the second case, the executor has taken away, because the guardianship of acting is for both, so when one acts in the right of his partner, it is not valid. Explanation: This is further explanation of the response from the two sides to Imam Abu Yusuf's analogy. They say there is a great difference: for the guardian, marrying the woman is a binding right, so if a suitable suitor proposes and the woman demands that the guardian marry her, it is obligatory upon him. Here there is no binding right upon the executor; rather he has an authority to act. That is why the executor has an option to act; it is not a binding right. So in the marriage case, when one brother performed the marriage, it is valid because it fulfilled a right that was obligatory upon his brother. In the second case, when one executor acts, it is not fulfilling a right of his partner, but rather seizing his partner's right, so it is not valid.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the authority of multiple executors (wasi) appointed in a will. The issue: when a testator appoints two executors, does each have independent authority to act alone, or must they act jointly? Imam Abu Yusuf argues that each executor has complete and independent authority. He provides three proofs: (1) Wasiyya (will) results in wilaya (guardianship), which is indivisible; thus each executor obtains full wilaya. He analogizes to two brothers who each have complete marriage guardianship over their sister. (2) Wasiyya is succession (khilafa); a successor inherits the same authority as the original, and the testator had full authority. (3) The testator's selection of both indicates equal affection, which is like kinship, and kinship grants full guardianship. The Sahibayn (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad) hold the opposite view: executors must act jointly. They argue that wilaya here comes by delegation (tafwid), and delegation is valid only as given. The testator delegated to two persons, so the condition of togetherness must be observed, as the testator was only satisfied with the pair. They rebut Abu Yusuf's analogy to marriage guardianship by pointing out a key distinction: marriage guardianship is based on kinship, which is equally complete in each brother, and it imposes a binding duty on the guardian to marry the woman upon her demand. In contrast, executorship grants only permission to act (not a duty), and each executor's authority is shared; acting unilaterally would usurp the other's right. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is the one requiring joint action unless the testator explicitly allowed independence. This passage is a classic example of Hanafi legal reasoning using qiyas (analogy) and tafriq (distinction).

Key Terms
وصایت (wasiyya): The institution of appointing an executor to manage one's estate after death.
ولایت (wilaya): Legal authority or guardianship granted to someone to act on behalf of another.
خلافت (khilafa): Succession; the executor stands in the place of the testator.
تفویض (tafwid): Delegation; the testator's act of entrusting authority to the executor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, if a testator appoints two executors without specifying independence, which statement is correct?
A) Each executor can independently sell estate property.
B) Both executors must act together for any transaction to be valid.
C) The executors must seek court approval for every action.
D) The eldest executor has priority and can act alone.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, multiple executors must act jointly by default; only the testator's explicit grant can allow independent authority.

Exceptions: Actions a Single Executor May Perform Alone
Enumeration of four exceptions to joint executor rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four exceptional acts a single executor may perform alone based on the Hanafi text and explain the reason of necessity for each.

Core Idea
A single executor may act alone only when delay threatens decay of the deceased or death of children—necessity overrides joint authority.

In typical cases, multiple executors must act jointly. However, Islamic law recognizes urgent situations where waiting could cause harm to the deceased or dependents. This passage from al-Quduri, as explained in the sharh, specifies four such exceptions: shroud, preparation of the body, and food and clothing for children. Understanding when necessity overrides general rules is essential for proper estate administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Recognize that executors normally must act jointly; this passage lists exceptions where a single executor may proceed alone.
2
Locate the four exceptions in the text
Find and list the four specific actions mentioned: purchasing shroud, preparing the deceased (tajhiz), providing food for children, and providing clothing for children.
The Arabic uses 'kafan', 'tajhiz', 'ta'am', and 'kiswah'.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each exception
For shroud and preparation: fear of decay (fasad al-mayyit). For food and clothing: fear of death of the children from hunger or exposure.
The text explicitly states 'لان فی التاخیر فساد المیت' and 'يخاف موته'.
4
Recognize the role of darurah (necessity)
Understand that necessity (darurah) permits a single executor to act without waiting, and even allows neighbors to intervene if the executor delays.
The principle 'al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat' is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كن كن چیزوں میں ایک وصی کے ہوتے ہوئے دوسرے کی انتظار نہیں کی جائے گی
فقال الا فى شراء كفن الميت و تجهيزه لان فى التاخير فساد الميت ولهذا يملكه الجيران عند ذالک و طعام ار و كسوتهم لانـ ــه يخاف موتهـ
غار
ترجمہ پس قدوری نے فرمایا مگر میت کا کفن خریدنے میں اور اس کی تجہیز میں اس لئے کہ تاخیر میں میت کا فساد ہے اس وجہ سے تجہیز کے بوقت تاخیر پڑوی مالک ہوں گے اور بچوں کا کھانا اور ان کے کپڑے خریدنے میں اس لئے کہ بھوکے اور ننگے ہونے کی وجہ سے بچوں کے مرجانے کا خوف ہے۔
تشریح اس عبارت میں چار چیزوں کا ذکر ہے - اگر میت کا کفن خریدنے کی ضرورت ہو تو ایک وصی خرید لے گا دوسرے کا انتظار نہیں کیا جائے گا کہیں ایسا نہ ہو کہ جب تک دوسرا آئے میت کی لاش سڑ جائے اس تاخیر کی وجہ سے پڑوسیوں کو بھی حق ہوتا ہے کہ وہ میت کے لئے کفن خریدیں۔
In which things will one executor not wait for the other? He (al-Quduri) said: Except in purchasing the shroud of the deceased and preparing him, because delay leads to corruption of the deceased; for this reason, at that time the neighbors may own it (the shroud). And (except in) their food and their clothing, because it is feared he will die. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Except in purchasing the shroud of the deceased and preparing him, because delay causes decay of the deceased; therefore at the time of preparation, the neighbors will have the right. And (except in) children's food and their clothing, because it is feared they will die. Explanation: In this sentence, four things are mentioned: if there is a need to buy a shroud for the deceased, one executor will purchase it without waiting for the other, lest the body decay while waiting. Due to this delay, neighbors also have the right to purchase a shroud for the deceased.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing exceptions to the general rule that multiple executors must act jointly. The author (al-Quduri's matn, commented on by the sharh) enumerates from the text of al-Quduri the cases where a single executor may act alone. The first two are purchasing a shroud (kafan) and preparing the deceased for burial (tajhiz). The reason is preservation of honor and avoidance of delay leading to decay (fasad al-mayyit). The third and fourth are providing food (ta'am) and clothing (kiswah) for minor orphan children or dependents under the executor's care, because delay could cause death from hunger or exposure. The sharh adds that these exceptions are based on necessity (darurah). It further notes that if one executor delays, even neighbors have the right to provide a shroud. The passage prepares the reader for a longer list (about fifteen items) but only mentions these first four explicitly. In Hanafi fiqh, these are understood as exceptions from the general principle of joint authority. The textual operation is parsing because the student must identify each specific exception and its justification.

Key Terms
کفن (kafan): The shroud or burial cloth for the deceased.
تجهیز (tajhiz): Preparation of the deceased for burial, including washing, shrouding, and other rites.
ضرورت (darurah): Necessity that makes an otherwise prohibited or restricted action permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why may a single executor purchase a shroud for the deceased without consulting the co-executor?
A) Because shrouding is a simple matter.
B) Because delay could cause the body to decay, which is a greater harm.
C) Because the co-executor has no right in the shroud.
D) Because the neighbor forces him.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a single executor may act alone only in four enumerated cases: shroud, preparation, children's food and clothing, because delay would cause greater harm.

Necessity Exceptions: When One Executor Must Act Alone
Lists the first three exceptions to joint executor rule based on necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first set of exceptions (kafan, food, clothing) and apply the principle that delay causing harm permits solitary action.

Core Idea
Where delay causes decay or death, one executor may act alone to prevent harm.

The general rule requires two executors to act jointly, but necessity overrides this rule. This lesson examines the first three exceptions: buying the shroud, preparing the body, and providing food and clothing for children. The underlying rationale is the fear of corruption or loss of life. Understanding these exceptions reveals how the Hanafi school uses necessity (ḍarūrah) to modify default rules.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Recall that joint executors (waṣiyyān) must act together in matters requiring guardianship (wilāyah). The passage begins by listing exceptions to this rule.
The default is joint action; exceptions are limited and justified.
2
Identify the first exception
The text states 'except in buying the shroud of the deceased and preparing him for burial.' Recognize that delay causes decay (fasād al-mayyit), which is a form of harm.
The justification is 'because delay causes decay of the deceased'.
3
Identify the second and third exceptions
The next exceptions are buying food and clothing for minor children (or dependents) because delay may lead to their death from hunger or exposure.
The text says 'because it is feared they may die' (خوف موتهم).
4
Analyze the underlying principle
The common thread is necessity (ḍarūrah) and the prevention of harm. The Hanafi school uses istiḥsān to allow solitary action when delay would cause corruption or loss of life.
This is a clear example of istiḥsān overriding qiyās.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: إلا في شراء كفن الميت وتجهيزه، لأن في التأخير فساد الميت، ولهذا يملكه الجيران عند ذلك، وطعامهم وكسوتهم، لأنه يخاف موتهم. ورد الوديعة بعينها، ورد المغصوب والمشترى شراء فاسداً، وحفظ الأموال، وقضاء الديون، لأنها ليست من باب الولاية، فإنه يملكه المالك وصاحب الدين إذا ظفر بجنس حقه، وحفظ المال يملكه من يقع في يده، فكان من باب الإعانة، ولا أنه لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي. وتنفيذ وصية بعينها، و عتق عبد بعينه، لأنه لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي. والخصومة في حق الميت، لأن الاجتماع فيها متعذر، ولهذا يتفرد بها أحد الوكيلين. وقبول الهبة، لأن في التأخير خوف الفوات، ولأنه يملكه الأم والذي في حجره، فلم يكن من باب الولاية.
He said: Except in buying the shroud of the deceased and preparing him (for burial), because delay causes decay of the deceased, and for this reason the neighbors may do it at that time; and (buying) food and clothing for them (the children), because it is feared they may die. And returning the specific trust, returning the usurped property and that bought with a corrupt purchase, preserving the assets, and paying the debts, because these are not from the category of guardianship (wilāyah); for the owner and the creditor own it when they obtain the same type as their right, and preserving the asset is owned by whoever gets it in his hand, so it is from the category of assistance, and because it does not require opinion. And executing a specific bequest, and manumitting a specific slave, because it does not require opinion. And litigation on behalf of the deceased, because gathering (of the executors) is difficult, and for this reason one of the two agents does it alone. And accepting a gift, because delay risks loss, and because the mother owns it and the one under her guardianship, so it is not from the category of guardianship.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage expands on the exceptions to the general rule that two executors (waṣiyyān) must act jointly. The commentator, drawing from the matn of al-Qudūrī, enumerates specific situations where one executor may act alone without waiting for the other. The underlying principle is that the general rule requiring joint action applies only to acts of guardianship (wilāyah) that require deliberation and opinion (ra'y). In contrast, the following acts are either matters of necessity (ḍarūra) where delay causes harm, or ministerial acts that do not require juridical reasoning. The list includes: (1) buying the shroud and preparing the deceased for burial, because delay would cause decay; (2-3) buying food and clothing for minor children, for fear of death from hunger or exposure; (4) returning a specific trust (wadīʿah); (5) returning a usurped item (maghsūb); (6) returning an item purchased through a corrupt sale (shirā' fāsid); (7) preserving the assets of the deceased (ḥifẓ al-amwāl); (8) paying debts (qaḍā' al-duyūn); (9) executing a specific bequest (tanfīdh waṣiyyah bi ʿayniha); (10) manumitting a specific slave (ʿitq ʿabd bi ʿaynih); (11) litigation (khuṣūmah) on behalf of the deceased, because it is impractical to gather both executors; (12) accepting a gift (qabūl al-hibah), because delay risks loss and because the mother (who often holds the gift) is not under guardianship. The commentator notes that these exceptions are based on necessity or the absence of need for opinion, and thus are not from the category of wilāyah. This is a clear example of istiḥsān (juristic preference) overriding qiyās.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): An executor appointed by the testator to manage the estate and carry out bequests after death.
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): A situation of extreme necessity that permits deviation from the general rule to prevent harm.
فساد (fasād): Corruption or decay; in this context, the decomposition of the deceased's body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Ḥanafī passage allow one executor to buy the shroud and prepare the deceased without the other?
A) Because buying a shroud does not require opinion.
B) Because delay would cause the body to decay.
C) Because the neighbors have a right to do it.
D) Because it is a form of worship.

Key Takeaway
Necessity to prevent immediate harm allows one executor to act alone in buying the shroud and providing food and clothing for dependents.

Acts Beyond Guardianship: When One Executor Can Return Trusts and Pay Debts
Lists exceptions based on absence of wilāyah: ministerial acts that one executor may perform alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts of guardianship (wilāyah) and ministerial acts (iʿānah), and apply the rule that the latter can be performed by one executor alone.

Core Idea
Acts that require no opinion or involve mere assistance are not guardianship; one executor may do them alone.

The passage continues to list exceptions that are not based on necessity but on the nature of the act itself. Returning a specific trust, paying a clear debt, or executing a specific bequest do not require deliberative judgment (ra'y). The Hanafi school categorizes these as acts of assistance (iʿānah) rather than guardianship (wilāyah). This lesson explores those exceptions and the underlying legal distinction.

Guided Path
1
Review the distinction between wilāyah and iʿānah
Wilāyah requires independent judgment and authority over another's affairs. Iʿānah is mere assistance or execution of a predetermined task.
The passage states: 'because these are not from the category of guardianship' (لأنها ليست من باب الولاية).
2
Identify the ministerial exceptions
List the acts that fall under iʿānah: returning a specific trust, returning usurped property, returning goods from a corrupt sale, preserving assets, paying debts, executing a specific bequest, manumitting a specific slave, litigation, and accepting a gift.
The text says these do not require opinion (لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي).
3
Analyze the rationale for litigation
Litigation is excepted because gathering both executors is impractical; the same applies to joint agents (wakīlayn).
The text says 'because gathering is difficult' (لأن الاجتماع فيها متعذر).
4
Understand the gift exception
Accepting a gift is excepted because delay risks loss, and also because the mother (who often receives the gift) is not under guardianship; thus it is not wilāyah.
The text says 'because delay risks loss' and 'because the mother owns it'.
5
Apply the distinction to new cases
If a task is purely ministerial (e.g., delivering a specific item), one executor may act alone. If it requires judgment (e.g., selling at a fair price), joint action is needed.
This is the key test: does the act require ra'y?

Six Matters Permissible for a Single Executor
Listing six situations where one executor may act alone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six matters a single executor can handle alone and explain the underlying principle of wilayah versus necessity.

Core Idea
A single executor may act alone in matters requiring no legal authority – only aid or necessity.

When a testator appoints two executors, the natural question arises: can one act without the other? The Hanafi school answers with a clear list of situations where joint action is unnecessary. This passage from al-Hidayah enumerates six such matters and grounds them in a single principle: they do not fall under the chapter of authority (wilayah) but rather under aid and necessity (i'anat wa darurah). Understanding these exceptions helps executors avoid unnecessary delays and ensures the deceased's affairs are settled efficiently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching principle
Read the passage to find the reason why these six matters are permissible alone. The author states: 'these matters are not related to the chapter of authority (wilayah)'. Locate this phrase and note the contrast with actions that do require joint authority.
The principle is that actions needing no delegated power (wilayah) can be done singly.
2
List the six matters
Extract each of the six situations mentioned. They include: (1) paying debts of the deceased, (2) executing a specific bequest, (3) emancipating a specific slave, (4) litigating on behalf of the deceased, (5) accepting a gift (hibah) for minors, and (6) protecting property. Mark each in the Arabic text.
The numbering in the text (10, 13) may be from the original list; count all distinct items.
3
Analyze each matter's rationale
For each matter, note why joint deliberation is not needed. For example, creditors can take their due without permission; litigation only one speaks; accepting a gift requires promptness to avoid retraction; protecting property is a natural duty. The passage provides analogies (owner of trust, creditor, invalid purchase).
The common thread: these acts do not benefit from multiple opinions or do not require delegated authority.
4
Contrast with actions requiring wilayah
Identify what would be the opposite: actions that do require joint action because they involve sale, substantial transactions, or decisions needing consensus. The text implies that such actions fall under 'bab al-wilayah' and require both executors.
This contrast helps define the boundary of the single executor's authority.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi position is that a single executor may act alone in these six matters. The Shafi'i school generally requires joint action unless the testator specified otherwise. Recognize this as a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.
Knowing the opposing view helps when advising clients or comparing fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسی طرح اگر میت پر قرضے ہوں تو ان کو ادا کرنے میں بھی دوسرے وصی کا انتظار نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ ان تمام امور کے تنہا جائز ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ یہ امور باب ولایت سے تعلق نہیں رکھتے کیونکہ جس نے موصی کی طرف سے ولایت حاصل نہیں کی ان امور کو وہ بھی انجام دے سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ جو مالک ودیعت ہے جب اس کو اپنا سامان مل جائے تو وہ خود اس کو لے سکتا ہے اسی طرح صاحب دین کو جب اپنے حق کی جنس مل جائے تو وہ اس کو خود لے سکتا ہے اور شراء فاسد میں بھی یہی حال ہے کہ مالک اس کو دے سکتا ہے۔
اور رہا مسئلہ حفاظت اموال کا تو اس کے لئے وصی ہی مخصوص نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ شخص جس کے ہاتھ میں یہ آجائے وہی اس کی حفاظت کر سکتا ہے تو یہ حفاظت بھی اعانت وضرورت کے باب سے ہے جس میں رائے کی کوئی حاجت نہیں ہوتی کہ یوں کہا جائے کہ دونوں کے جمع ہو جانے سے رائے میں مضبوطی پیدا ہو جائے گی۔
-۱۰ کوئی معین وصیت ہو اس کو نافذ کرنے میں دوسرے وصی کا انتظار کرنے کی ضرورت نہ ہوگی۔ اور کوئی معین غلام کا عشق ہو اس کے لئے بھی جنسی کے انتظار کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ ان میں بھی رائے کی کوئی
ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ کسی شخص سے میت کے حق میں خصومت کرنی ہو تو ایک وصی کر سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ خصومت تو دونوں کے جمع ہونے کی صورت میں بھی ایک ہی وصی کرے گا تا کہ دونوں کے مجلس قضاء میں جمع ہونے کی وجہ سے شور و شغف نہ ہو، اس وجہ سے اگر کسی کے دو وکیل ہوں پھر بی حصومت ایک ہی کرے گا۔ ۱۳ اگر کوئی بچوں کے لئے ہبہ کرے تو ایک وصی اس کو قبول کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ اگر وہ دوسرے وصی کا انتظار کرنے لگے تو ہوسکتا ہے کہ ہبہ کرنے والا پھر ہبہ نہ کرے اس وجہ سے یہ اختیار ماں کو بھی دیا گیا ہے کہ وہ بچہ کے لئے مہیہ کو قبول کرے اور اسی طرح وہ شخص بھی بہہ کو قبول کر سکتا ہے جس کی زیر پرورش بچہ ہو، بہر حال میبہ کو قبول کرنا ولایت کے باب سے نہیں ہے۔
Similarly, if the deceased has debts, then in paying them, the other executor will not be waited for. The reason for all these matters being permissible alone is that these matters are not related to the chapter of authority (wilayah), because a person who has not obtained authority from the testator can also perform these matters; for the owner of a trust, when his own goods become available, he can take them himself; similarly, a creditor, when he finds the item of his right, he can take it himself; and in an invalid purchase (shira' fasid), the same applies that the owner can give it. As for the matter of protecting wealth, the executor is not exclusive for it; rather, the person in whose hands it comes can protect it. So this protection is also from the chapter of aid and necessity, which does not require opinion in a way that it could be said that by gathering both, strength in opinion would be produced. (10) If there is a specific bequest (wasiyyah), there is no need to wait for the other executor to execute it. And if there is an emancipation of a specific slave, there is no need to wait for the other executor, because in these also there is no need for opinion. (13) If someone makes a gift (hibah) for the children, one executor can accept it, because if he starts waiting for the other executor, it may happen that the donor does not give the gift. For this reason, this authority has also been given to the mother that she can accept a gift for the child, and similarly, the person under whose guardianship the child is can also accept the gift. In any case, accepting a gift is not from the chapter of authority (wilayah).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school listing six situations where a single executor (wasi) can act without waiting for a co-executor. The overarching principle is that these actions do not require legal authority (wilayah) from the testator; they are either natural rights (like the owner of deposited goods taking them back), acts of necessity (like protecting property), or actions that do not benefit from joint deliberation (like litigation or accepting a gift). The commentary explains each: (1) paying debts of the deceased – because the creditor has a right to take his due; (2) executing a specific bequest or emancipating a specific slave – because these are determined and do not require opinion; (3) litigation on behalf of the deceased – because even if both executors were present, only one would speak to avoid confusion; (4) accepting a gift for minors – because delay may cause the gift to be retracted; (5) protecting property – because any person with possession can safeguard it; (6) collecting scattered property – mentioned later. The author emphasizes that these are not from 'wilayah' but from 'aid and necessity' (i'anat wa darurah).

Key Terms
وصی (wasi): The executor appointed in a will (wasiyyah) to manage the testator's affairs after death.
باب ولایت (bab al-wilayah): The chapter of legal authority; actions that require the jurisdiction or delegated power from the testator, often needing joint deliberation.
ہبہ (hibah): A gift; a transfer of ownership without consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a single executor accept a gift (hibah) for a minor without the co-executor?
A) Because accepting a gift requires legal authority (wilayah), and one executor has partial authority.
B) Because accepting a gift is an act of necessity and does not require joint deliberation; delay may cause the gift to be lost.
C) Because the testator appointed both executors only for major transactions.
D) Because the minor's mother can also accept, so the executor is not needed.

Key Takeaway
A single executor may act alone in matters of debt payment, specific bequests, slave emancipation, litigation, gift acceptance, and property protection, because these are not from the chapter of authority but from aid and necessity.

Selling Perishables and Collecting Scattered Property by a Single Executor
Ruling and proof for a single executor's action regarding perishable and scattered assets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a single executor may sell perishable goods and collect scattered property, and explain the proof based on necessity (darurah).

Core Idea
Necessity permits a single executor to act alone to prevent loss.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario in estate administration: when the deceased leaves perishable items or scattered assets. The Hanafi school holds that a single executor—even if multiple were appointed—may take immediate action without waiting for co-executors. The proof rests on the principle of necessity (darurah) and the fact that such acts are not exclusive to executors, as even strangers can perform them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Recognize that the act is selling perishable goods and collecting scattered property. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) for a single executor to do so alone.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is based on necessity (darurah) – delaying would cause loss. Additionally, since even a stranger can collect scattered property to preserve it, this is not an act requiring delegated authority (wilayah).
3
Analyze the rationale
Necessity overrides the general rule that multiple executors must act jointly. The text states 'because there is a necessity that is not hidden' – the urgency is obvious.
4
Apply to examples
Consider the commentary's examples: milk from buffaloes or goats that must be sold before spoiling; scattered cash or goods that need to be gathered quickly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع ما يخشى عليه التوى والتلف لان فيه ضرورة لا تخفى وجمع الاموال الضائعة لان في التاخير خشية الفوات ولانه يــمــلــكـــــه كـــل مــن وقع في يده فلم يكن من باب الولاية
ترجمہ اور اس چیز کو بیچنا جس کی ہلاکت اور تلف ہو جانے کا خوف ہو اس لئے کہ اس میں ضرورت ہے جو پوشیدہ نہیں ہے اور متفرق اموال کو جمع کرنا اس لئے کہ تاخیر میں فوات کا خوف ہے اور اس لئے کہ اس کا ہر وہ شخص مالک ہے جس کے ہاتھ میں واقع ہو جائے تو یہ ولایت کے باب سے نہ ہوگا۔
تشریح
-۱۴- وہ اشیاء کہ جن کے ضائع ہو جانے اور بگڑ جانے کا اندیشہ ہو جیسے مثلاً بھینس اور بکریوں کا دودھ ہے تو اس کو ایک وصی فروخت کر سکتا
ہے اس لئے کہ اس میں ضرورت ہے۔
۱۵- اگر موجی کا مال متفرق ہو تو اس کو ایک وصی جمع کر سکتا ہے ورنہ فوت ہو جانے کا خوف ہے اور چونکہ اس کام کو تو میت کے پڑوسی بھی کر سکتے ہیں تو وصی بدرجہ اولی کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ اس کا تعلق باب ولایت سے نہیں ہے۔
And selling what is feared to be lost and wasted because there is a necessity that is not hidden, and collecting scattered properties because in delay there is fear of loss, and because every person in whose hand it falls owns it, so it is not from the chapter of authority. Translation: And selling the thing which is feared to be destroyed and wasted, because there is a necessity which is not hidden. And collecting scattered properties because in delay there is fear of loss, and because every person in whose hand it falls owns it, so this will not be from the chapter of authority. Explanation: (14) Those items whose loss and decay are feared, such as the milk of buffaloes and goats, then one executor can sell it because there is necessity in it. (15) If the testator's wealth is scattered, then one executor can collect it, otherwise there is fear of loss. And since even the neighbors of the deceased can do this work, the executor can do it more rightfully because it is not related to the chapter of authority.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof from the Hidayah's matn (base text) and its commentary. The ruling: A single executor may sell items that are likely to spoil or be lost (e.g., perishable milk) and may collect scattered property of the deceased. The proof is based on necessity (darurah) – delay causes certain loss. Additionally, since any person who finds such property could take it for safekeeping (as a finder), it is not an act of wilayah (delegated authority) but rather an act of preservation. The commentary gives examples: selling buffalo or goat milk before it spoils, and gathering dispersed assets to prevent them from being lost. The rationale is that even a stranger could do these acts out of necessity, so the executor, being a fiduciary, can do them alone.

Key Terms
التوى والتلف (al-tawa wal-talaf): Loss and destruction; the risk of property being ruined or perishing.
جمع الاموال الضائعة (jam' al-amwal al-da'i'ah): Collecting scattered or lost properties of the estate to prevent loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason that a single executor can sell perishable goods without the co-executor?
A) Because the testator only appointed one executor for such tasks.
B) Because delaying sale would lead to loss, and necessity allows immediate action.
C) Because the co-executor is not available.
D) Because the executor has full authority over all property.

Key Takeaway
A single executor may act alone to sell perishables and collect scattered property when delay would cause loss, justified by necessity.

Joint Action of Executors for Sale and Collection
Ruling from Jami' al-Saghir on joint action of executors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a single executor cannot sell or collect debts, and explain the two legal proofs from Jami' al-Saghir.

Core Idea
A single executor cannot sell or collect debts; the testator's trust was in both collectively, and collection is an exchange requiring authority.

In this passage from al-Jami' al-Saghir, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani establishes a key principle of joint executorship: acts of sale and taking possession (taqadi) require both executors to act together. This ruling is rooted in the testator's intention and the nature of these acts as exchanges. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly administering estates according to Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Explicit Ruling
Read the opening statement: 'It is not permissible for one of the two executors to sell or to collect.' Recognize that this is an unambiguous prohibition (la yajuz) from a foundational Hanafi text.
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah, the most authoritative layer of Hanafi transmission.
2
Understand the Key Term Taqadi
Note that taqadi means taking possession (qabd) of a debt or property. The text clarifies that it refers to the act of collecting or receiving payment. Recognize that this is not merely a physical act but a legal transfer.
In the custom of Kufa, taqadi was used specifically for collection of debts.
3
Analyze Proof 1: Testator's Joint Trust
The first reason given is that the testator was satisfied with the trustworthiness of both executors jointly for taking possession. This implies that the testator's confidence was in their combined action, not individual action.
The testator's intent (raḍā) is a key consideration in wajiyyah rulings.
4
Analyze Proof 2: Exchange Nature of Taking Possession
The second reason is that taking possession is in the meaning of exchange (mubadalah), especially when the types differ. Acts of exchange fall under the chapter of authority (wilayah), which requires delegated power. Since a single executor does not have full authority over such exchanges, joint action is necessary.
Even when goods are of the same type, possession still carries an exchange element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تشریح جامع صغیر میں ایک جزئیہ پیش کیا گیا ہے کہ اگر زید نے دو شخصوں کو اپنا وصی مقرر کیا تھا اور زید کے مرنے کے بعد ان میں سے کوئی ایک موصی کے مال کو فروخت کرنا چاہتا ہے یا موصی کے مال پر قبضہ کرنا چاہتا ہے جو کسی پر ہے تو ایک کو اس کا حق نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ زید نے ان دونوں کی اجتماعی امانت پر اعتماد کیا ہے اور اسی سے وہ راضی ہوا تھا۔
دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس کا تعلق باب ولایت سے ہے اس لئے کہ یہ امور مستثناۃ کے اندر داخل نہ ہونگے اس لئے کہ قبضہ میں بہر مورت مبادلہ کے معنی پائے جائیں گے اتحاد جنس کی صورت ہو تب بھی اور اختلاف جنس کی صورت میں تو اس کا مبادلہ ہونا امر بدیہی ہے اس لئے کہ ایک کو اس کا حق نہ ہوگا کہ وہ دوسرے کے بغیر فیصلہ کرے یا فروخت کرے۔
And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: It is not permissible for one of the two executors to sell or to collect (taqadi). And by taqadi is meant taking possession (iqitida'). This is what is meant in their custom. And this is because the testator was satisfied with the trustworthiness of both of them jointly in taking possession. And because it is in the meaning of exchange, especially when the types differ, as known, so it is from the chapter of authority. Translation: And in Jami' al-Saghir it is stated: One of the two executors does not have the right to sell or to take possession. And by taqadi is meant taking possession. This is what is meant in the custom of the people of Kufa. And this is because the testator was satisfied with the trustworthiness of both of them in connection with taking possession. And because taking possession is in the meaning of exchange, especially when the types differ, according to the detail that has been known, so it will be from the chapter of authority. Explanation: In Jami' al-Saghir, a case is presented that if Zaid appointed two persons as his executors, and after Zaid's death, one of them wants to sell the testator's property or wants to take possession of the property that is in someone's hand, then one does not have this right because Zaid relied on the collective trust of both and he was satisfied with that. The second reason is that it is related to the chapter of authority, because these matters will not fall within the exceptions, because in taking possession, the meaning of exchange is found in any case; even in the case of identical types, and in the case of different types, its being exchange is self-evident. Therefore, one does not have the right to decide or sell without the other.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Jami' al-Saghir (one of the six core books of Imam Muhammad) stating that a single executor cannot sell property or take possession (taqadi) of debts/assets. The proof is twofold: (1) The testator trusted both executors collectively for taking possession, so one alone cannot fulfill that trust. (2) Taking possession (qabd) is in the nature of exchange (mubadalah), which requires legal authority (wilayah). Since sale and exchange of property are acts of authority, they fall under the general rule requiring both executors, unless explicitly excepted. The commentary explains that even when the goods are of the same type (e.g., exchanging one dirham for another), possession still has an exchange element, so it requires joint action. The exception for perishables and scattered property (previous lessons) are specific exceptions.

Key Terms
تقاضی (taqadi): Taking possession (qabd) of a debt or property from a debtor; it means to receive payment or collect a claim.
باب الولاية (bab al-wilayah): The chapter of legal authority; actions that require delegated power from the testator, often requiring joint action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the testator only trusted both executors together for that task.
B) Because taking possession is a physical act that requires two people.
C) Because the debtor may not accept payment from one executor.
D) Because the executor must first consult the heirs.

Key Takeaway
A single executor cannot sell or collect debts without the other; both must act jointly because the testator's trust was in both collectively and these acts are exchanges requiring authority.

Separate Appointment of Executors: Independence or Joint Action?
Dispute on independent vs. joint action in separate executor appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on separate appointment of executors and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of each.

Core Idea
Separate appointment may grant independence, but some Hanafi scholars equate it to joint appointment because the will vests at death.

When a testator appoints two executors separately (each named individually without a joint condition), may each act alone? This passage from al-Hidayah records an intra-Hanafi dispute. Two opinions are reported, each with a different analogy: one likens executors to independent agents, the other emphasizes that executorship becomes binding only at death, so all appointees share the same starting point. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of the distinction between agency (wakalah) and executorship (wasiyyah).

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the two opposite rulings: (1) each executor acts independently, (2) both must act jointly. Note that both are attributed to the Hanafi school ('qila').
The first view is based on the analogy with separate agents (wakil).
2
Examine the first proof
The first position argues that separate appointment means the testator is satisfied with each one's opinion individually. Just as two separately appointed agents can act independently, so can executors.
3
Examine the second proof
The second position argues that the obligation of the will (wujub al-wasiyyah) arises only at death, so all executors are simultaneously vested with authority. Agency, on the other hand, can be successive (tata'aqab), so the analogy fails.
The key distinction: agency can be sequential, executorship is simultaneous.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the first view, a single executor can sell property without the other; under the second, both must consent for major dispositions.
The outcome affects estate administration and liability.
5
Assess the tarjih
Al-Marghinani does not explicitly choose, but the later preferred view (mufta bihi) leans towards independence, analogizing to separate agents. However, caution may require joint action for significant transactions.
The tarjih is not fully settled; later Hanafi manuals often treat separate appointment as giving independent authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أوصى الى كل واحد على الانفراد قيل يتفرد كل واحد منهما بالتصرف بمنزلة الوكيلين اذوكل كل واحد على الانفراد وهذا لانه لما افرد فقد رضی برای الواحد وقيل الخلاف في الفصلين واحد لان وجوب الوصية عند الموت بخلاف الوكيلين لان الوكالة تتعاقب
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے ہر ایک کو الگ الگ وصی بنایا ہو تو کہا گیا ہے کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک تصرف کے سلسلہ میں متفرد ہو گا دو وکیلوں کے درجہ میں جب کہ ہر ایک کو الگ الگ وکیل بنایا ہو اور یہ اس لئے کہ جب موصی نے افراد سے کام لیا تو وہ ایک کی رائے سے راضی ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ دونوں صورتوں میں ایک ہی اختلاف ہے اس لئے کہ وصیت کا وجوب موت کے وقت ہے بخلاف دو وکیلوں کے اس لئے کہ وکالت تو آگے پیچھے ہوتی ہے۔
And if he appointed each one separately, it is said: each one of them will be independent in disposition, like two agents when each is appointed separately. And this is because when he used separation, he is satisfied with the opinion of one. And it is said: the difference in the two cases is one, because the obligation of the will is at the time of death, unlike two agents, because agency (wakalah) occurs successively. Translation: And if he appointed each one separately, it is said that each one of them will be independent in disposition, in the status of two agents when each one is appointed separately. And this is because when the testator used separation, he is satisfied with the opinion of one. And it is said that the difference in both cases is one, because the obligation of the will is at the time of death, unlike two agents, because agency occurs successively.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether separate appointment of executors allows each to act independently. Two views are recorded: (1) 'Qila' (it is said) – each executor is independent like separate agents (wakil), because the testator expressed satisfaction with one opinion. (2) Another 'qila' – the ruling is same as joint appointment (i.e., both must act together) because the will becomes obligatory at death, unlike agency where appointments can be sequential. Thus, the difference between joint and separate appointment is disputed. The text does not explicitly resolve which view is preferred, but the general principle in the Hanafi school is that joint appointment requires joint action; separate appointment may allow independent action, but caution leans towards joint action for major transactions. The commentary in al-Hidayah may later clarify the tarjih.

Key Terms
الانفراد (al-infirad): Separate appointment; the testator names each executor individually without conditioning joint action.
تتعاقب (tata'aqab): Occur successively; used for agency (wakalah) where one agent can be replaced or act after another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion in this passage, why is separate appointment of executors treated like joint appointment?
A) Because the testator's trust is in both collectively as the will becomes binding at death.
B) Because the executors are like agents who act successively.
C) Because the appointment is separate but the testator changed his mind.
D) Because the executor must consult the heirs regardless.

Key Takeaway
The timing of vesting (death) distinguishes executorship from agency and affects whether separate appointment grants independent authority.

Two Executors Appointed Separately: Can Each Act Alone?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on solitary action of separately appointed executors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on solitary action of a separately appointed executor and explain the key distinction between wasiyya and wakala.

Core Idea
In wasiyya, separate appointments become joint at death; in wakala, timing matters.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether an executor (wasi) appointed separately from another can act alone. The resolution hinges on the moment at which the appointment takes effect — at death for wasiyya, during life for wakala. Understanding this distinction clarifies the divergent rulings of Imam Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The testator appoints two executors separately, not jointly. The question: can one executor act alone without the other?
This is a common scenario in estate planning.
2
Note the ruling of Faqih Abu al-Layth
He holds that each executor may act alone because the testator's separate appointment is evidence of satisfaction with each individual's opinion.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn's view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) prohibit solitary action regardless of whether the appointment was joint or separate, because the wasiyya is established only at death, making both appointments simultaneous and joint.
Note that Abu Yusuf himself is part of the Sahibayn but dissents on this specific issue.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf permits solitary action because the testator's separate appointment shows satisfaction with each alone. However, his view is that the wasiyya is still joint at death, but the testator's intention overrides the default jointness.
5
Analyse the distinction from wakala
In wakala (agency), appointments take effect during life and accept succession (ta'aqub), so separate appointments are effective at different times. In wasiyya, appointments take effect only at death, so separate appointments are simultaneous, making them effectively joint unless the testator's intention says otherwise.
This distinction is key to understanding the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا
۲۵۲
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۶ اختیار ہے، اسی طرح فقیہ ابواللیث وغیرہ حضرات کا کہنا ہے کہ جب موصی نے دو وصی مقرر کئے لیکن دونوں کو ایک ساتھ وھی نہیں بنایا بلکہ ہر ایک کو الگ الگ وصی بنایا ہے تو ہر وصی کو اختیار ہو گا کہ وہ اپنے ساتھی کی عدم موجودگی میں تصرف کرے کیونکہ جب موصی نے ان کو الگ الگ وصی بنایا تو یہ اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ وہ ایک کی رائے سے راضی ہے، اور بعض حضرات نے کہا ہے کہ موصی خواہ دونوں کو ایک ساتھ وہی بنائے یا الگ الگ وصی بنائے دونوں صورتوں میں ایک ہی حکم ہے یعنی طرفین کے نزدیک یہ جائز نہیں ہے کہ ایک وصی تنہا تصرف کر دے اور امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک جائز ہے اور وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ وصیت اجتماعا ہویا انفرادا دونوں کا حال ایک ہے اس لئے کہ وصیت کا ثبوت تو بوقت موت ہوتا ہے تو دونوں صورتوں میں وصیت کے اندر تو اجتماع ہی ثابت ہوگا۔
البتہ تو کیل کے اندراجتماع و افراد کارگر ہوگا اس لئے کہ وکالت تعاقب کو قبول کرتی ہے یعنی جس کو مؤکل نے جس زمانہ میں وکیل بنا دیا تو وہ وکیل ہو گیا لیکن وصیت کا ثبوت بوقت موت ہی ہوتا ہے۔
Book of Wills. Page 252. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu Commentary on Hidayah Volume 16. It is a matter of choice. Similarly, Faqih Abu al-Layth and others said that when the testator appointed two executors but did not make them joint, rather made each one a separate executor, then each executor has the option to act in the absence of his partner, because when the testator appointed them separately, it is evidence that he is satisfied with the opinion of one. And some scholars said that whether the testator makes them joint or separate, the ruling is the same in both cases, i.e., according to the two (Sahibayn), it is not permissible for one executor to act alone, and according to Imam Abu Yusuf it is permissible. The reason for this is that the wasiyya (bequest) is established at the time of death, so in both cases, jointness is established in the wasiyya. However, in agency (wakala), the distinction between joint and separate is effective, because agency accepts succession, i.e., whenever the principal appoints an agent, he becomes an agent. But the proof of wasiyya occurs only at the time of death.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the authority of two executors appointed separately (not jointly). The issue is whether each executor can act alone in the absence of the other. According to Faqih Abu al-Layth and others, if the testator appoints them separately, each may act alone because the testator's satisfaction with each individual's opinion is implied by the separate appointment. However, some scholars hold that the ruling is the same as joint appointment: neither can act alone, according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf himself, however, permits solitary action in the case of separate appointment, arguing that the wasiyya is established only at death, so in both cases the appointment is effectively joint (since both appointments are confirmed simultaneously at death). The passage then distinguishes this from wakala (agency), where the timing of appointment matters because agency accepts succession: the principal can appoint an agent at any time, so joint vs. separate appointments have different effects. In wasiyya, the appointment takes effect only at the testator's death, so separate appointments become simultaneous joint appointments.

Key Terms
وصی (wasiy): An executor appointed by the testator (musi) to carry out the will and manage the estate after death.
وصیت (wasiyya): A bequest or will, the act of appointing an executor or making a testamentary disposition.
وکالت (wakala): Agency, where the principal (muwakkil) appoints an agent (wakil) to act on his behalf during his lifetime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why can't an executor appointed separately act alone?
A) Because the testator's separate appointment means he does not trust each alone.
B) Because wasiyya is established only at death, so separate appointments become joint.
C) Because the executor must always seek court approval.
D) Because the executor can only act jointly as per the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction between wasiyya and wakala is that wasiyya takes effect only at death, making separate appointments simultaneous, while wakala accepts succession, preserving the timing of separate appointments.

The Reason for Appointing a New Executor: Incapacity or Intention?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the rationale for qadi appointing a new executor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that the qadi appoints a replacement when one of two executors dies, and explain the two Hanafi rationales (incapacity vs. intention).

Core Idea
When one of two joint executors dies, the qadi appoints a replacement—but the Hanafi imams differ on why.

This passage addresses a scenario where one of two joint executors (wasiyyan) dies, and whether the qadi should appoint a replacement. All Hanafi imams agree that he must, but they dispute the reasoning. Understanding this internal debate deepens your appreciation of how legal reasoning (ta'lil) operates even when the outcome is the same.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed ruling
All imams agree that when one of two joint executors dies, the qadi appoints another executor in his place. This is the operative ruling (hukm).
The appointment is wajib upon the qadi to protect the deceased's estate.
2
State the first rationale (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two'), the remaining executor is legally incapable of acting alone because the original appointment was joint. Therefore, the qadi must add a second executor to enable valid transactions.
This rationale is based on the principle that joint executors are one unit; the death of one incapacitates the other.
3
State the second rationale (Abu Yusuf)
Abu Yusuf holds that the remaining executor is capable of acting alone, but the testator's original intention was to have two representatives. To fulfill that intention, the qadi appoints a replacement.
Even though Abu Yusuf considers the remaining executor capable, the appointment is necessary to realize the testator's intent.
4
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
Both rationales lead to the same practical outcome: the qadi appoints a new executor. The disagreement is purely theoretical (ikhtilaf fi al-ta'lil).
No difference in fatwa; both positions are accepted within the school.

If one of the two executors dies, the qadi appoints another executor in his place. Sayings of the jurists: 'If one of them dies, the qadi appoints another executor in his place. According to the two (Sahibayn), because the remaining one is incapable of acting alone, so the qadi adds another executor to him, out of consideration for the deceased when he is incapacitated. According to Abu Yusuf, the living one among them, even if he is capable of acting, the testator intended that two persons should be his representatives in his rights, and that is possible to achieve by appointing another executor in place of the deceased.' Translation: So if one of the two executors dies, the qadi appoints another executor. According to the two (Sahibayn), because the remaining is incapable of acting alone, the qadi joins another executor with him, out of kindness to the deceased when the remaining is incapacitated. According to Abu Yusuf, the living one among them, even if he is capable of acting, the testator intended that two persons should be his representatives in his rights, and this intention can be realized by appointing another executor in place of the deceased. Explanation: If Zayd had appointed two executors, but one of them died, the qadi appoints another in his place. According to the two, the reason is clear: the single executor is incapable of acting alone, so the qadi adds another with him, which is better for the deceased. However, there is an issue with Imam Abu Yusuf: according to him, why should another be appointed when he holds that one executor is capable of acting? He said that the reason is that the testator intended that two persons should be his representatives, and this intention can be fulfilled by appointing another in place of the deceased.

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where one of two joint executors (wasiyyan) dies, and whether the qadi should appoint a replacement. All parties agree that the qadi should appoint another executor, but they differ on the reasoning. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the remaining executor is unable to act alone because the original appointment was joint; therefore, the qadi appoints a second to enable valid transactions. According to Abu Yusuf, even though he holds that a single executor can act alone (as seen in the previous lesson), the testator's original intention was to have two representatives; thus, to fulfill that intention, the qadi appoints a replacement. The Urdu commentary clarifies that according to the 'two' (likely Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but in context it might refer to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad? Actually the text says 'طرفین' which likely means 'the two sides' or 'the two imams' referring to the Sahibayn as opposed to Abu Hanifa? This needs careful handling. The Arabic says 'عندهما' which typically means 'according to the two' (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and then 'وعند ابي يوسف' separately. So the dispute is between the Sahibayn and Abu Yusuf alone? That is odd as Abu Yusuf is one of them. Possibly 'عندهما' refers to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad? But standard Hidayah usage: 'عندهما' = Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. This may be an error in the text. The Urdu says 'طرفین' which could mean 'the two parties' i.e., the majority? Regardless, both positions lead to the same outcome: the qadi appoints another.

Key Terms
قاضی (qadi): The Islamic judge who has authority to appoint an executor when the original one dies, to protect the deceased's estate.
عاجز (ajiz): Incapable; the remaining executor is considered legally incapable of acting alone after the death of his co-executor.

Can the Living Executor Act Alone? Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Succession of Authority
Ruling on surviving executor's authority when deceased appointed him as his own executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on a surviving executor acting alone when the deceased co-executor appointed him as his own executor, and explain the legal reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The living executor representing the deceased's opinion creates a dual authority, allowing him to act alone.

This lesson examines a specific scenario where a testator appointed two executors, one of whom dies and appoints the surviving co-executor as his own executor. The Hanafi school has a dispute: according to the relied-upon view (zahir al-riwaya), the surviving executor may act alone because he now embodies both opinions. However, a narration from Abu Hanifa holds that he cannot, because the testator was satisfied only with the joint opinion of the two original individuals. Understanding this nuance clarifies the importance of the number of effective opinions in estate management.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Legal Question
The testator (Zayd) appoints two executors (Khalid and Bakr). Khalid dies, but before death appoints Bakr as his own executor. Can Bakr now act alone in managing Zayd's estate?
2
State the Zahir al-Riwaya Position and Its Reasoning
According to the authoritative transmission (zahir al-riwaya), Bakr can act alone. The reason: Khalid's appointment transfers his authority to Bakr, so Bakr now represents both his own and Khalid's opinions. Thus, the testator's requirement for joint opinion is satisfied.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
3
State the Alternative Narration from Abu Hanifa and Its Reasoning
In a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that Bakr cannot act alone. The reason: the testator was only satisfied with the joint opinion of the two specific individuals he appointed (Khalid and Bakr), not with Bakr alone even if he inherits Khalid's authority.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Note the Practical Consequence and the Preferred View
If Bakr can act alone, the qadi does not need to appoint another executor. If not, the qadi must appoint a replacement. The preferred view is the zahir al-riwaya position because it gives effect to the deceased executor's appointment and avoids unnecessary qadi intervention.
The distinction also applies if Khalid had appointed a third person: then Bakr must act jointly with that third person to satisfy the dual opinion requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دو وصیوں میں ایک وصی فوت ہو جائے اور اس فوت ہونے والے نے بھی زندہ وصی کو اپنا وصی بنایا ہوتو وہ اکیلا تصرفات کا حق رکھتا ہے
ولزان الميت منهما اوصى الى الحى فللحى ان يتصرف وحده فى ظاهر الرواية بمنزلة ما اذا اوصى الى شخص اخر ولا يحتاج القاضى الى نصب وصى اخر لان رأى الميت باق حكما برأى من يخلفه و عن أبي حنيفة انه لا ينفرد بالتصرف لان الموصى ما رضى بتصرفه وحده بخلاف ما اذا اوصى الى غيره لانه ينفذ تصرفة برأى المثنى كما رضية المتوفى
ترجمہ اور اگر دونوں وصیوں میں سے جو مرا ہے اس نے زندہ وصی کو وصی بنایا ہو تو زندہ کے لئے حق ہے کہ وہ تنہا تصرف کرے ظاہر الراویہ کے مطابق اس صورت کے درجہ میں جب کہ اس نے کسی دوسرے شخص کو وصی بنایا ہو اور قاضی دوسرا وصی مقرر کرنے کا محتاج نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ میت کی رائے اس کے خلیفہ کے ذریعہ حکما باقی ہے اور ابو حنیفہ سے منقول ہے کہ زندہ تنہا تصرف نہیں کر سکتا اس لئے کہ موصی تنہا اس کے تصرف سے راضی نہیں ہوا ہے۔ بخلاف اس صورت کے جب کہ وصی نے زندہ کے غیر کو وصی بنایا ہو اس لئے کہ زندہ کا تصرف نافذ ہو گا دو کی رائے ہونے کی وجہ سے جیسا کہ میت (موسی) اس سے راضی ہوا تھا۔
تشریح زید نے خالد اور بکر دونوں کو اپنا وصی مقرر کیا تھا ان میں سے خالد کا انتقال ہو گیا اور خالد نے بوقت انتقال بکر کو اپنا وصی مقرر کر دیا تھا یعنی اپنے اختیارات اس کو تفویض کر دئیے تھے تو ظاہر الروایہ کے مطابق بکر تنہا تصرفات کا حق رکھتا ہے اور اب قاضی کو دوسر اوسی مقرر کرنے کی حاجت پیش نہیں آئے گی اس لئے کہ خالد کا خلیفہ بکر موجود ہے تو اس خلافت کی وجہ سے خالد کی رائے بھی مود سے تو حکما بکر کی رائے ڈبل رائے ہے۔
تو یہ ایسا ہی ہو گیا جیسے جب کہ خالد کسی دوسرے شخص کو اپنا ہی مقرر کرتا تو جائز تھ اسی طرح اگر بکر کو کر دے تو بھی جائز ہے۔ اور بروایت حسن بن زیاد امام ابو حنیفہ سے منقول ہے کہ بکر کو تنہا تصرفات کا اب بھی حق حاصل نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ زید تنہا بکر کی رائے سے راضی نہیں ہوا تھا بلکہ وہ دو کی رائے سے راضی ہوا تھا۔
البتہ اگر خالد نے بکر کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے کو اپنا وصی مقرر کیا تو جائز ہے کہ بکر اس کے ساتھ مل کر تصرف کرے اس لئے کہ اب بکر تنہا نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ دو شخص ہیں اور یہی موصی کا مقصد تھا یعنی وہ دو شخصوں کی رائے سے راضی ہوا تھا۔
If one of the two executors dies and the deceased one also appointed the living executor as his own executor, then the living one has the right to act alone. In the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), it is like the case where he appointed someone else, and the qadi does not need to appoint another executor because the opinion of the deceased remains legally through the opinion of his successor. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he cannot act alone, because the testator was not satisfied with his action alone. Unlike the case where he appointed someone other than the living executor, because his action will be effective due to having two opinions, as the deceased was satisfied with that. Explanation: Zayd had appointed Khalid and Bakr as his executors. Khalid died and at the time of death appointed Bakr as his executor, i.e., delegated his authority to him. According to zahir al-riwaya, Bakr has the right to act alone, and the qadi does not need to appoint another because Bakr is the successor of Khalid, so Khalid's opinion is legally present, making Bakr's opinion double. So it is as if Khalid had appointed someone else; similarly, appointing Bakr is valid. And in a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Imam Abu Hanifa, Bakr still does not have the right to act alone because Zayd was not satisfied with Bakr's opinion alone, but rather with the opinion of two. However, if Khalid had appointed someone else besides Bakr, then it is permissible for Bakr to act together with that person, because now Bakr is not alone but there are two persons, and that was the testator's intention: he was satisfied with the opinions of two persons.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: the testator appointed two executors, one of them (Khalid) dies, but before death he appoints the surviving co-executor (Bakr) as his own executor. According to zahir al-riwaya (the relied-upon transmission of the Hanafi school), the surviving executor (Bakr) may now act alone in managing the original testator's estate. The reason is that Khalid's appointment of Bakr effectively transfers Khalid's authority to Bakr, so Bakr now represents both his own opinion and Khalid's opinion (by virtue of being Khalid's executor). This makes Bakr's decision equivalent to a joint decision of two persons, which satisfies the original testator's intent for joint management. Therefore, the qadi does not need to appoint another executor. However, a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Imam Abu Hanifa held the opposite view: Bakr cannot act alone because the testator (Zayd) was only satisfied with the joint opinion of his two appointed executors, not with Bakr alone, even if Bakr inherits Khalid's authority. The passage also explains that if Khalid had appointed a third person (not Bakr) as his executor, then Bakr could act together with that third person, because then there would be two individuals, satisfying the original testator's intent for dual opinion. This lesson highlights the principle that the number of effective opinions matters, not just the persons.

Key Terms
ظاہر الروایہ (zahir al-riwaya): The apparent or well-known transmission of the Hanafi school, considered the authoritative position.
خلیفہ (khalifa): Successor; the living executor is considered the representative of the deceased executor's authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to zahir al-riwaya, why can the living executor act alone when the deceased co-executor appointed him as his own executor?
A) Because the living executor now has the authority of both his own and the deceased's opinion.
B) Because the testator originally appointed only one executor.
C) Because the qadi has no authority in such matters.
D) Because the deceased executor's family gave permission.

Key Takeaway
A living executor can act alone after the deceased co-executor appointed him as his own successor, because the deceased's opinion is legally present through the successor, satisfying the testator's requirement for joint opinion.
